> So thanks, Teresa. So my name is Emmanuel Gisa, and I'm the Africa Director for the Center for Digital Public Infrastructure. Wonderful. So tell us, Emmanuel, what does this center do? So CDPI is a pro bono tech advisory facility housed within IIITB, Bangalore. And what we help, we do is to help countries to design better systems and ensure that DPI is widespread across the world. It's a global South-South initiative. And one of the key areas where we intervene is in sitting with governments and supporting them to design solutions that are open, interoperable, and privacy by design. So electronic know your customer is a way to digitally verify the identity of a customer that you're engaging and that you're working with. And that's for a bank. And it is for a bank primarily. But in Zambia, we've had an expanded use case and scope. And so we have one of the digital health platforms using the same electronic KYC platform. We also have the government's social protection fund for the drought victims, victims of the worst drought in Zambia in 100 years. And so that drought intervention program is utilizing this same electronic KYC to help with identification of beneficiaries and also disbursement of the – yeah. And so it makes it easier for the government to be able to provide the funding to students in universities primarily, and also in Tivet colleges. Tivet has technical vocational training centers. So we're talking about electronic KYC, but let's back up just a little bit, because when we – you're here discussing digital public infrastructure, and that's one important component of it. Yes. But if we peel back the layer and just start at the most foundational level for DPI, a country needs to also have strong power and internet before you can even get there. Yes. So can you comment in Zambia, did you already find that foundation in place? So we did, and it's interesting that you mentioned this. I think it takes us back to the whole philosophical conversation around DPI and what to do when you're looking at how best to – what are the prerequisites for a successful deployment. And the irony is that Zambia's actually had their worst drought in 100 years, by extension because their power generation is hydro. They've also then had to contend with serious number of hours in a day of blackout. And then you load on top of that that impact of the drought and power outage on connectivity, and you have a real case for DPI not to work, right? In the same sense, I think the intentionality behind it, the drive behind it has meant – one of the things that's happened is we've had sort of a country that is so committed against all odds to make tech work. So for example, they very early on on the continent adopted Starlink. Right after Nigeria, it was Zambia that took on Starlink and made sure that there's coverage across the country. They have some incredible government-driven infrastructure solutions. One is called Infratel, which is the telecoms company that has towers across the country, and the widest spread of towers in Zambia is actually driven from the government-owned Infratel, which runs as a separate entity subsidiary. And on the power side, they've had to prioritize some of those critical service areas. So there's been very minimal disruption. And then to sort of mitigate against all that power problem, they've had to partner with China, of course, to do very large-scale solar intervention for the country. And so solar for the first time has been brought on board and has been made available as wide as possible within the country, which is a great learning for them. So these are not DPI questions, but they are obviously sort of, to say, a very intentional approach to making sure that you're actually delivering for your DPI needs, which will require power and they'll require internet and they'll require stability of the same. So to that extent, yes, one of the philosophical elements is that DPI requires connectivity to thrive. But beyond that philosophical element, I think DPI also requires political will and drive to thrive. So the successes, yes, there's the infrastructure, the physical rails that it runs upon, but it's also the powerful political statement of champions that push the DPI agenda. I want to follow up with one of the comments you made about connectivity. Do you have a view that we can achieve these DPI objectives over wireless, or do you think that we have to have broadband cable in order to have the speeds necessary to achieve the objectives? I think the evidence actually points to possibilities of wireless being what you need. So at the risk of being too Zambia-centric, it's a vast country with sparse population sat there. There is Tanzania as well. It's a vast country, again, very concentrated population centers to the contrary. And many other countries that have had to adopt wireless. Starlink has demonstrated much higher speeds than most of the fiber connectivity solutions that are available within most metro fiber ecosystems in the continent. And so I do believe that the wireless wired gap is narrowing. We are starting to see just as good decent speeds on wireless as we have had on fiber in the metros. So this is, to my mind, I think it's possible. There are other elements around it. I mean, issues around access, which to my mind comes down to cost, comes down to elements around technology applied to access. So are you using USSD, for example? Is that technology appropriate for the context that you're working with, or is the technology more driven by the smartphone type applications? And what I would say there is that we still have to figure out the need to have appropriate tech for this population. So where you have feature phone access, for example, it allows you to drive some of this adoption at a very low level in the rural areas where you don't only have low connectivity rates, but you also have low access to smartphone devices. So I think the smart devices could be a distraction. The over-emphasis on the same, because you may not then get to a level and scale that you may want. But if you can build DPI technologies that still work on USSD or work on a simple feature phone, as was the case with mobile money, for example, which is scalable and appropriate even where you apply assisted tech for the communities. Those are some of the thought processes that I think on the Africa side, it's nuanced. So one of the questions that people had was, what is the difference between Africa and India in that instance? And I think at the risk of preempting it, I think there's a possibility to oversimplify the comparisons between the two areas. But two main ones, I think the fact that in India, it's one country. In Africa, you're dealing with 54 different countries, 40 different currencies. And you're also dealing with a not so widespread, cheap access to smartphone and towers and internet, when in India already, they had free calls by certain points in their DPI journey. Yeah. I have so many questions for you. You've said so many. Your comments are so rich, and we're so privileged to speak with you today. Coming back to this question around the essentials of power and connectivity, do you think that it makes sense for African governments to be thinking about various applications before they have achieved the goals that they need to achieve relative to power and connectivity? In other words, does it need to be sequenced? Or should another investment be made in the applications before investments are made in advancing power and connectivity? Do you have to get power and connectivity in place first? So it depends on how you perceive it. And I think it's interesting because in the African context, there's the view we hold, or many people hold, that, oh my God, vast parts of this continent are not covered. OK? But from a GSMA study, I think the question should be framed differently. There is the coverage gap, usage gap, and access gap. And so if you think about it from a coverage gap standpoint, the gap has pretty much narrowed in many ways, whereby for the most part, many countries already have. The dark spots have reduced. I mean, the spaces without any connectivity at all. And that leaves you with usage, which is a factor of cost and affordability, and access, which is a factor of device or lack thereof of devices. And so I think that the most important elements for us to consider is to almost scale the stairs and chew gum at the same time for most of these countries, because it's a case of you can't be left behind. You can't have your young people growing up in a space, an environment that doesn't empower and provide tech for them. But at the same time, you can't be in an environment that is in the dark, so without power. So it's almost an imperative. I think it also creates that sense of urgency is helpful, because it creates creativity. The necessity of it breeds creativity so much so that as you set up your power infrastructure, you need to combine it with fiber that runs along that power line so that as you're reaching the homes, you're actually delivering a triple or dual play. So you're either landing in electricity and fiber for internet, but you could also be landing educational content. So you're landing so much more. And if you think about how we have evolved through the 90s into the new millennia and all these things, you then almost start to think and feel that we leapfrogged the whole telephone, wired telephony era and just jumped. And all of a sudden, about 400 million Africans then got to be connected on the telephone when previously you had maybe less than 5 million telephones in homes. They may be building with the assumption that all that is sorted out. So take this solution or leave it. But we have an opportunity to build based on that local understanding, local wiring, local ability to say, if I have to walk to school, how can I build a solution that helps that child who's walking to school be safe as they walk to school and also to be able to alert the village in case of danger. Such kind of solutions only come from your own understanding of the reality on the ground. Where is Kenya in all of this? Wow, wow, Teresa. I think Kenya's had good leadership on tech, but I think that's been taken for granted. I think that it's almost digressed to my mind because there's a sense to which talent exists and there's great talent, but there is a gap in leadership. Remember what I talked about the role of leadership in driving forward adoption and even scale of DPI. You need leadership. I think there is a sense to which Kenya, in the space we are in at the moment, as DPI is taking off, we are not getting the leadership in the right spaces that can then help us to navigate, can help the younger people to build solutions. I attended a very illuminating hackathon two months back and I was quite impressed. I'd become very discouraged that, oh my God, where's all this talent? Where did it go? The young people were very switched on. I was so impressed by the technology, the solution that was coming out, but then I said there's a gap because there can't be this many solutions that are being brought forth by these young people. It was a hackathon and they were supposed to build something in 24, actually, no, just over 24 hours, maybe 36 hours. They came up with some really, really solid solution. Needless to say, they were more oriented around civic engagement and all that, but I was quite impressed at their speed, at how many there were. I mean, this hackathon, we didn't expect more than 50, but there was, I think, 90 enrollments that came through from the developer community. I said, this is impressive, just needs to be harnessed. If I cast my mind at the time when the leadership that Kenya provided on tech and all these things existed, I think there was a great sense to which political will, political leadership and ministerial levels were very much switched on. I mean, there's a change of guard now and our hope is they probably will take a bit more forward-looking approach to harness all this talent that existed in the country for greater good. Manuel, thank you so much. You are clearly one of the continent's leaders in this space and it's our privilege to be able to sit down and speak with you and learn about your views on DPI in Africa today. Thank you so much. And then you load on top of that, that impact of the drought and power outage on connectivity, and you have a real case for DPI not to work, right? In the same sense, I think the intentionality behind it, the drive behind it has meant one of the things that's happened is we've had sort of a country that is so committed against all odds to make tech work. So for example, they very early on on the continent adopted Starlink. Right after Nigeria, it was Zambia that took on Starlink and made sure that there's coverage across the country. They have some incredible government-driven infrastructure solutions. One is called Infratel, which is the telecoms company that has towers across the country, and the widest spread of towers in Zambia is actually driven from the government-owned Infratel, which runs as a separate entity subsidiary. And on the power side, they've had to prioritize some of those critical service areas. So there's been very minimal disruption. And then to sort of mitigate against all that power problem, they've had to partner with China, of course, to do very large-scale solar intervention for the country. And so solar for the first time has been brought on board and has been made available as wide as possible within the country, which is a great learning for them. So these are not DPI questions, but they are obviously sort of, to say, a very intentional approach to making sure that you're actually delivering for your DPI needs, which will require power and they'll require internet and they'll require stability of the same. So to that extent, yes, one of the philosophical elements is that DPI requires connectivity to thrive. But beyond that philosophical element, I think DPI also requires political will and drive to thrive. So the successes, yes, there's the infrastructure, the physical rails that it runs upon, but it's also the powerful political statement of champions that push the DPI agenda. I want to follow up with one of the comments you made about connectivity. Do you have a view that we can achieve these DPI objectives over wireless, or do you think that we have to have broadband cable in order to have the speeds necessary to achieve the objectives? I think the evidence actually points to possibilities of wireless being what you need. So at the risk of being too Zambia-centric, it's a vast country with sparse population sat there. There is Tanzania as well. It's a vast country, again, very concentrated population centers to the contrary. And many other countries that have had to adopt wireless. Starlink has demonstrated much higher speeds than most of the fiber connectivity solutions that are available within most metro fiber ecosystems in the continent. So I do believe that the wireless wired gap is narrowing. We are starting to see just as good decent speeds on wireless as we have had on fiber in the metros. So this is, to my mind, I think it's possible. The other elements around it, I mean, issues around access, which to my mind comes down to cost, comes down to elements around technology applied to access. So are you using USSD, for example? Is that technology appropriate for the context that you're working with? Or is the technology more driven by the smartphone type applications? And what I would say there is that we still have to figure out the need to have appropriate tech for this population. So where you have feature phone access, for example, it allows you to drive some of this adoption at a very low level in the rural areas where you don't only have low connectivity rates, but you also have low access to smartphone devices. So I think the smart devices could be a distraction. I over-emphasize on the same because you may not then get to a level and scale that you may want. But if you can build DPI technologies that still work on USSD or work on a simple feature phone, as was the case with mobile money, for example, which is scalable and appropriate even where you apply assisted tech for the communities. Those are some of the thought processes that I think on the Africa side, it's nuanced. So one of the questions that people had was, what is the difference between Africa and India in that instance? And I think without the risk of preempting it, I think there's a possibility to oversimplify the comparisons between the two areas. But two main ones, I think the fact that in India, it's one country. In Africa, you're dealing with 54 different countries, 40 different currencies, and you're also dealing with a not so widespread cheap access to smartphone and towers and internet when in India already they had free calls by certain points in their DPI journey. Yeah. I have so many questions for you. You've said so many. Your comments are so rich, and we're so privileged to speak with you today. Coming back to this question around the essentials of power and connectivity, do you think that it makes sense for African governments to be thinking about various applications before they have achieved the goals that they need to achieve relative to power and connectivity? In other words, does it need to be sequenced? Or should another investment be made in the applications before investments are made in advancing power and connectivity? You have to get power and connectivity in place first. So it depends on how you perceive it. And I think it's interesting because in the African context, there's the view we hold, or many people hold, that, oh my God, vast parts of this continent are not covered. Okay? But there's, from a GSMA study, I think the question should be framed differently. There's the coverage gap, usage gap, and access gap. And so if you think about it from a coverage gap standpoint, the gap has pretty much narrowed in many ways, whereby for the most part, many countries already have the dark spots have reduced. I mean, the spaces without any connectivity at all. Then that leaves you with usage, which is a factor of cost and affordability, and access, which is a factor of device or lack thereof of devices. And so I think that the most important elements for us to consider is to almost scale the stairs and chew gum at the same time for most of these countries, because it's a case of you can't be left behind. You can't have your young people growing up in a space, an environment that doesn't empower and provide tech for them. But at the same time, you can't be in an environment that is either dark, so without power. So it's almost an imperative. I think it also creates that sense of urgency is helpful because it creates, it breeds creativity. The necessity of it breeds creativity so much so that as you set up your power infrastructure, you need to combine it with fiber that runs along that power line so that as you're reaching the homes, you're actually delivering a triple or dual play. So you're either landing in electricity and fiber for internet, but you could also be landing educational content. So you're landing so much more. And if you think about how we have evolved through the 90s into the new millennia and all these things, you then almost start to think and feel that we leapfrogged the whole telephone wired telephone era and just jumped and all of a sudden about 400 million Africans then got to be connected on telephone when previously you had maybe less than 5 million telephones in homes. So I think that there's a possibility there that whereas, yes, we do not have access in many ways to electricity or the electricity access is unstable. These provide opportunities for smarter devices that use solar to charge them and smarter devices that allow for homes to be wired and connected through solar powered lighting or schools to be connected through solar powered lighting. And all these opportunities then present more scope, faster speed to access. Yeah. Another question from something you said, you'd mentioned how we mustn't be distracted by the lack of access to smartphones and that there's a fair bit you can do without them with just feature phones. And you gave a very good example of mobile money, which started in your home country in Kenya with Mpesa. Can you give some other examples of applications that one can use on feature phones without having the need to have a smartphone? Because that's a very effective one that you mentioned. Yeah. So I think that there's been the power of financial services. I think DFS is one area that we've witnessed lots of access and transformation from even if you discount for mobile money, I mean, even just mobile banking, you know, where interaction between the bank and, you know, the customer issues on two factor authentication, where you can still implement security features of various applications and services using any type of phone. If you look at Ag, which is another space where applications can be accessed using USSD. And so by dialing a short code, you're able to receive either information or closes AgroVet or share information about your harvest and how much you have available for sale for the market and so on and so forth. So there's lots of innovation that is still possible to be implemented using feature phones and USSD sort of low level tech. I think I'm not saying that there's no use in place for smartphones and all that stuff. But I think the notion that you need all of these things before you get started, then means that, oh, let's just wait or let's just, let's look for $50 billion master plan to get Africa having the devices that they need to then move forward, which I think is problematic because we also discount for all these possibilities that we may be dealing with literacy levels that are still low. You're dealing with much more enhanced power needs, you're dealing with much more enhanced connectivity needs, because all the smartphones come with their own demand and drain on data. Do you then extrapolate it to cost and the cost are just way out of reach for the average person trying to balance between the  for food and maybe $3 or $4 for 1,000 MB of data. I like what you're saying, which is another way of saying, don't let great be the enemy of good. Yes. If we can do some good, do it. We can get started. I mean, if the digital financial sector focused on, oh, my God, we have to have Java-enabled funds before we do anything, then we'd never have moved the needle on access to financial services. Let's talk a little bit about African empowerment in terms of this whole DPI movement. I think you have some interesting thoughts there. Share some thoughts. Yeah, yeah. So thanks for that. I think the reality we have here is that nobody's coming to save Africa, in a sense. I think we are in a very interesting time as a continent where I think belief in self, belief in our own abilities to have answers to our problems. From mobile money's rise and rise, we can see that, okay, the continent needed solutions. We know as a continent what solutions would work for us. We know where the chute pinch is, in a sense. So my view is that we need to continuously build tech and DPI that responds to our needs. My dream is to see more and more of our young entrepreneurs, young developers building solutions for this continent with their context in mind, because someone from outside may never have the context that we are even dealing with access issues or connectivity issues. They may be building with the assumption that all that is sorted out. So take this solution or leave it. But we have an opportunity to build based on that local understanding, local wiring, local ability to say, if I have to walk to school, how can I build a solution that helps that child who's walking to school be safe as they walk to school, and also to be able to alert the village in case of danger? Such kind of solutions only come from your own understanding of the reality on the ground. And I think my hope is for the continent that we begin to have that belief in ourselves, believe that we can do it. One last question for you. As a Kenyan, Kenya has often been considered the Silicon Savannah of Africa. We've talked about what's happening in other countries. Tell us a little bit about where you see DPI in Kenya, because we know that there's a lot of development there, a lot of entrepreneurial activity, a lot of high-tech skills. Where is Kenya in all of this? Wow, wow, Teresa. I think Kenya's had good leadership on tech, but I think that's been taken for granted. I think that it's almost digressed to my mind, because there's a sense to it that because there's a sense to which talent exists, and there's great talent, but there is a gap in leadership. Remember what I talked about, the role of leadership in driving forward adoption and even skill of DPI. You need leadership. I think there is a sense to which Kenya, in the space we are in at the moment, as DPI is taking off, we are not getting the leadership in the right spaces that can then help us to navigate, can help the younger people to build solutions. I attended a very illuminating hackathon two months back, and I was quite impressed. I had become very discouraged that, oh my God, where's all this talent? Where did it go? The young people were very switched on. I was so impressed by the technology, the solution that was coming out, but then I said, there's a gap, because there can't be this many solutions that are being brought forth by these young people. I mean, it was a hackathon, and they were supposed to build something in 24, actually, no, just over 24 hours, maybe 36 hours, and they came up with some really, really solid solution. Needless to say, they were more oriented around civic engagement and all that, but I was quite impressed at their speed, at how many there were. There were, I mean, this hackathon, I mean, we didn't expect more than 50, but there was, I think, 90 enrollments that came through from the developer community, and I said, this is impressive, just needs to be harnessed. And if I cast my mind at the time when the leadership that Kenya provided on tech and all these things existed, I think there was a great sense to which political will, political leadership and ministerial levels were very much switched on. I mean, there's a change of guard now, and our hope is they probably will take a bit more forward-looking approach to harness all this talent that exists within the country for greater good. Manuel, thank you so much. You are clearly one of the continent's leaders in this space, and it's our privilege to be able to sit down and speak with you and learn about your views on DPI in Africa today. Thank you so much. Thank you. Thank you.

They may be building with the assumption that all that is sorted out. So take this solution or leave it. But we have an opportunity to build based on that local understanding, local wiring, you know, local ability to say, if I have to walk to school, how can I build a solution that helps that child who's walking to school, you know, be safe as they walk to school, and also to be able to alert the village in case of danger. Such kind of solutions only come from your own understanding of the reality on the ground. And I think my hope is for the continent that we begin to have that belief in ourselves, believe that we can do it. One last question for you. As a Kenyan, Kenya has often been considered the Silicon Savannah of Africa. We've talked about what's happening in other countries. Tell us a little bit about where you see DPI in Kenya because we know that there's a lot of development there, a lot of entrepreneurial activity, a lot of high-tech skills. Where is Kenya in all of this? Wow. Wow, Teresa. I think Kenya's had good leadership in tech, but I think that's been taken for granted. I think that it's almost digressed to my mind because there's a sense to which talent exists and there's great talent, but there is a gap in leadership. Remember what I talked about, the role of leadership in driving forward adoption and even scale of DPI. You need leadership. I think there is a sense to which Kenya in the space we are in at the moment, as DPI is taking off, we are not getting the leadership in the right spaces that can then help us to navigate, can help the younger people to build solutions. I attended a very illuminating hackathon two months back and I was quite impressed. I had become very discouraged that, oh my God, where's all this talent? Where did it go? The young people were very switched on. I was so impressed by the technology, the solution that was coming out, but then I said there's a gap because there can't be this many solutions that are being brought forth by these young people. I mean, it was a hackathon and they were supposed to build something in 24, actually, no, about just over 24 hours, maybe 36 hours. And they came up with some really, really solid solution. Needless to say, they were more oriented around civic engagement and all that. But I was quite impressed at their speed, at how many they were. They were, I mean, this hackathon, I mean, we didn't expect more than 50, but there was, I think, 90 enrollments that came through from the developer community. And I said, you know, this is impressive. Just needs to be harnessed. And if I cast my mind at the time when the leadership that Kenya provided on tech and all these things existed, I think there was a great sense to which political will, political leadership and ministerial levels were very much switched on. I mean, there's a change of guard now and our hope is they probably will take a bit more forward-looking approach to harness all this talent that existed in the country, you know, for greater good. Manuel, thank you so much. You are clearly one of the continent's leaders in this space and it's our privilege to be able to sit down and speak with you and learn about your views on DPI in Africa today. Thank you so much. Thank you. Thank you.








   2. Introducing DAAS – DPI as a Packaged Solution  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6bbr5pcSkyQ](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6bbr5pcSkyQ) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6bbr5pcSkyQ&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> So, one of the important learning that we went through as the DPI, Digital Public Infrastructure, rollout happened in the countries is that various reasons, for various reasons because most of these projects are driven by some government departments or ministries. So obviously, the rigor of the paperwork and the transparency reasons and so on, government takes more time. But peculiarly, when it comes to Digital Public Infrastructure, which is supposed to be a set of minimal building blocks, not the full solution, on top of which other people can build. Even there, even though they are minimal and simple, in one sense, lot of us think they are simple enough, frankly. But it does take a long time, long time due to variety of reasons. Reasons such as, you know, who is going to help me as if I am a department, who is going to put together the first set of documents to kick start the project. So I need a consultant or I need somebody, but how do I choose a consultant? Then I need an RFP to choose a consultant. So I already spent two, three months to choose a consultant or a partner. When one starts the project, then projects, you have to define the entire requirements and the, you know, contractual obligations around it in the form of an RFP to actually build the DPI. So, it is like saying, oh, I want to build my house. Obviously, I have to make up my mind of everything in the house, design the whole house, everything, then outsource it to someone to build it. Now, this custom way of doing it has two problems. One, you have to know upfront what you want to build. So you take a long time to figure out what do you actually want, what do you want to build, then use a mechanism to write all that down into an RFP process. So typically preparation of this RFP itself and requirements, contractual obligations, SLAs and the functional requirements, technology requirements, scale and security requirements all have to be spelt out in a government RFP and they will spell it out, it takes months. Then you put out an RFP, then you hope that you have competitive bids coming from the, you know, ecosystem. The problem with that is by the time you get through this funnel of figuring out, you selecting a vendor, it already probably at least nine months, six to nine months, maybe even 12 months. So when you select that, you remember, you have not even began writing anything or building no house yet, nothing has been built yet, not even the first brick has been laid yet. You are in the process of that, you already spent nine months. Now you are saying, okay, now I have to onboard the new vendor, system integrator or somebody who I selected, they start building the house, building the DPI. And the build typically, because everything is custom, the build takes slightly longer because they tried building everything. When you build something, you have to test it out, you have to do security testing, functional testing, acceptance, payment of the vendor appropriately. And that cycle, what's called a build test, get ready cycle is also another minimum. No software vendor is going to say, I'll build it for one week. It is going to be typically because system integrators take time, they do say everything is in months. So another six, nine months, so by the time you are already one and a half years, by the time you get to some form of rollout, then you test it out production ready rollout, right, that is what you do. So typical cycle of what we call a 24 month, you know, cycle that goes on. Question we were asking is this, how can we compress this? Because every leader in a country wants it faster. Bureaucrats who are running, who are in charge of the business, wants it faster. It's not their interest to get stuck in a technology build cycle with no outcome at the end of the tunnel. So it's important that we compress the cycle because leadership in the countries want faster rollout of DPIs and compressed timelines. Interestingly, citizens also want it because they're also been waiting. So you see this tremendous friction between the speed and agility wanting to do that fast versus the traditional IT procurement, large project overhead that is always there for a large DPI. So as part of many conversations with countries, this idea evolved over the last few months, the idea called DAS. And DAS stands for DPI as a package solution. Package solution is a real keyword is that can we reimagine at least the popular DPIs, not all DPIs, like identity, credentialing, data, payment, you know, things that everybody talks about, registries and many other pieces of this puzzle that now most world over people have understood that being the most common, what's called a foundational set of building blocks. These foundational DPIs, there is no reason, frankly, to rebuild them from scratch. Much of that is available in digital public goods as open source projects. So the real question we were asking is can we take those projects? Can we package the DPI? DPI, not only software, technology packaging, policy packaging, project plan packaging, fund packaging, can we package everything as much as possible? Now we know obviously you can't package it 100% but package it so that the contextualization into a country's, you know, the mapping between the package and the actual country deployment is from two year cycle, which we talked about. We can compress it to three to six months. And finally, we actually believe it's totally possible, very, very, very possible for us to, if we put all our heads together, we should be able to very clearly take the common set of foundational DPGs, package them fully, including policy templates, project plan templates, training, capacity building, ecosystem readiness. If we can package that well, there is no reason why countries should go through an elongated high risk two year, three year cycles for DPI deployment. So DAS provides a brilliant way to bridge the gap between the agility and the speed of the leaders that leaders want to see to open source way of fast tracking this DPI implementation through DPGs, package DPGs. So the DAS is the name that actually brings this idea forward. And it can be cloud native, cloud ready, private cloud, public cloud, it doesn't matter where you deploy, but it's one click deployable. Think of that very fast deployable, rapid piloting and rapid policy shift, then allow the countries to continue to own their IP, their data, their management control. There is no lack of any of that, but except that we are just compressing the two year time.

   4. Enabling Sustainable Development with Digital Public Infrastructure  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VpVI8jI\_de4](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VpVI8jI_de4) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?pp=0gcJCdgAo7VqN5tD&v=VpVI8jI_de4&utm_source=chatgpt.com)

**Video Transcript:**
> The adoption of digital tools in everyday life is accelerating worldwide. Across the developing
> world, governments' success in adapting to these changes can make or break their country's
> development journey. What is the modern infrastructure that one needs to run a state in a digital
> era? I think there's a politically contested notion about what those range of things are, and not
> all countries agree, and that set of things may vary from place to place. But I think at its very
> minimum core is you need to have some form of identification system, you want some autonomous way
> to ensure transactions in your country so that, you know, your economy can't be shut down by a
> third actor, and you want some sort of way to issue credentials and enable like e-signatures or
> some way to execute contracts in a way that's secure. The bigger piece here is you're not getting
> out of the 21st century without those three things being in place. This collection of systems is
> the essence of what we call Digital Public Infrastructure or DPI. The term DPI gained prominence
> during India's G20 presidency in 2023. There is no universal operating standard for DPI, allowing
> countries to develop it in different ways to best fit their own needs. However, the core components
> of DPI consist of digital identification, a digital payment system, and a data exchange system.
> Good DPI is safe, secure, and interoperable. It is built on open standards and accessible by
> everyone. It facilitates streamlined interactions between citizens and their governments to deliver
> efficient and citizen-centric goods while reducing bureaucracy and enhancing transparency. DPI
> comes in the picture at this point because it's really trying to be a response to trying to unlock
> innovation, more healthy competitive markets. It's trying to create systems of trust and shared
> value out of data. Good DPI can help countries meet their development goals, including closing the
> gender-digital divide. Achieving gender equity remains a challenge because women still face many
> barriers in the digital economy. For example, women disproportionately lack access to a
> government-issued ID, especially in developing countries. In 2021, approximately 43% of adult women
> in low-income countries did not have a government-issued ID compared to only 2% in advanced
> economies. This makes it difficult to open and use accounts in financial institutions, secure
> employment, and purchase assets such as a mobile phone. Women are also more limited in accessing
> financial services and making digital payments, and overall have lower levels of digital literacy
> than men. A DPI built by women and with women in mind can be a driver of change, extending from the
> household level up through entire societies. Digital can be a really incredible tool, but I also
> see when we look at the backsliding that is happening, we can either intentionally use it to
> accelerate the pace of change, or there's a risk that we see that these tools will actually grow
> different divides and could exacerbate different challenges. And so we really want to be thoughtful
> and smart about how we leverage the tools for health goals and for our development goals and for
> women's economic power in particular. To maximize the transformative impact of DPI, the
> international community needs to elevate and broaden the dialogue around it. CSIS looks forward to
> continuing to advance the DPI conversation to expand economic growth and women's economic
> empowerment in developing countries.

   5. DPI in Education | Shankar Maruwada (CEO & Co-founder – EkStep)  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6LaMn5si03w](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6LaMn5si03w) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=6LaMn5si03w&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> What is Digital Public Infrastructure? Imagine a 3 year old girl going to school for the first time. It takes 15 years for that little girl to be transformed into a confident teenager, by the time she is complete with her school education. That 15 years is a complex set of actors, parents, teachers, administrators, content creators, academicians, community, technologists, whole lot of people come together, the classmates of that girl, all of them come together to make it an education journey for that 3 year old to become an 18 year old. In a country like that, we have 260 million such children, learning in 22 languages, across 60 boards of education, each autonomous, taught in 1.4 million schools, taught by 9.5 million teachers. When you are faced with something as large, as diverse, as complex as this, the question we asked as X-Step Foundation, when we were set up in 2015, was what is the role technology can play in solving for education outcomes? That thinking led us to the DPI approach, and some of us were anyway, part of the Aadhaar startup journey, under the leadership of Nanda Nilekani, who is also my co-founder at X-Step Foundation with Rohini Nilekani. So we knew how DPIs work. The question was, how could we use DPIs in education? And if you think about it, the 3 key constructs of DPI, which is a strong digital backbone or infrastructure, appropriate policies, and innovation by the ecosystem, are even more relevant in education. And that is how we conceived of a digital infrastructure for education. In fact, it is called DIGSHA, which is Digital Infrastructure for Knowledge Sharing, X-Step Foundation co-created it with the Government of India and many other players. It was launched in around December 2017 as offering access to local content. So what it did was, when the Government of India offered this as an infrastructure to all those 60 boards, each board could create their own digital content and link it to the existing textbooks. What this did was, it allowed children to access digital content relevant to them, relevant to the topic they are learning, through the mechanism of unique QR codes in the textbook. So as a result, 600 million plus textbooks all over India became QR coded and connected to digital content, each created by their respective boards. And as part of implementing the national education policy, where digital infrastructure is a crucial component of technology. What this did was, over 17, 18, 19, and before the pandemic of 2020, there was a mass scale adoption. More than 30 states and union territories out of India's 36, came on board this digital infrastructure offered by the Government of India. So DIGSHA was a Government of India infrastructure, X-Step and some of the other not-for-profits came together and co-created this. This infrastructure, on which the various other boards innovated on, in their own language, with their own content, and in line with the broad policy architecture of the national education policy 2020, is again a very good example of digital public infrastructure in education. But what is DPI? It is that which offers a specific service at population scale, so that others don't have to create that service. Because the Government of India provided this national infrastructure as a service, the various boards could now use that service to link their existing physical content in textbooks, in their language, with their curriculum, to the relevant digital content. Innovation on top. And all in line with existing policies. So this was the first major use case of DPI, and an example of innovation by the ecosystem on top. The second example of why DIGSHA is a good DPI is it is evolvable. And I'll give you two examples of that. During pandemic, when schools shut down, teacher training also shut down. Learning by children continued, because those textbooks were already QR coded, and children could access that digital content sitting at home. But how do teachers come together and train? So very quickly, another service was added on DIGSHA, which is teacher training. And because DIGSHA was designed as a DPI, and using the building blocks, very rapidly, a teacher training building block was added. As a result, over the next two, three years, 22,000 courses have been created by the ecosystem. And close to 140 million teacher credentials have been issued. Around 7 million teachers undergoing these courses and getting the 140 million credentials. Another part of a good DPI is inclusive innovation. In DIGSHA, it did not matter what kind of a device you had, whether it was a mobile phone, a laptop, a tablet. And if a child did not have access to a digital device, the teachers, and most teachers in India have a device, the teachers could showcase that content in class, either through televisions, like in certain states, or in some cases, through their own personal mobile phones, which has happened in other states. So thinking of inclusive innovation while creating a DPI was a necessity for a country as large and diverse as India. But that's not enough of a DPI, because no DPI is born perfect and fully complete. A good DPI can and must keep evolving. So when the generative AI disruption hit us all in 2022, there was a great opportunity for DIGSHA to leverage that and add a generative AI component into DIGSHA. So very quickly, another service was added, wherein a combination of generative AI on Indian languages and using open source digital building blocks came together to create a series of bots for teachers, for parents, where they could access relevant information about how to teach better for teachers, about how to handle children, young children at home for parents. And these answers are in the local language of the teacher or the parent. And they refer to official documents, which are mostly in English or Hindi, so that the answers are authoritative and also relevant to the question. So this is a good example of the evolvability of a DPI and how DIGSHA was designed for evolvability. The other aspect of a DPI is, does it allow the ecosystem to work better together? As I explained, education is a complex ecosystem. There are so many people working together. A good DPI should be able to help them work better. And a way to do that is through responsible data sharing, data on usage of content, data on credentialing, which teachers have got which credentials on completing courses. And DIGSHA was designed for sharing of data responsibly within the system by those who needed that data. It could be data about course completion, data about which content in which language of which subject was being used more, so that the authorities can take better decisions on the back of that. In the case of DIGSHA, what was different was, it was built on a set of open source code that AXSTEP put together, what we call as digital public goods. In this case, it is Sunbird. What a well-designed digital public good does for DPIs is it allows the creation of multiple DPIs. So while DIGSHA was created using Sunbird, another learning platform called IGOT was also created. This one by the Department of Personal and Training and specifically for training government employees. Two different DPIs built of the same digital public good. Why is this important? When other countries want to create their own DPI in education, they don't need to start from scratch. They can take openly available, free to use public good like Sunbird and create their own DIGSHA. And there's a lot of interest and a lot of efforts going on to take Sunbird by other countries and build their own learning platforms, their own DPIs on top. And that brings me back to a central concept that can differentiate a great DPI from a good DPI, which is the idea of open innovation. In education in particular, we have seen islands of excellence. We have seen silos of innovation. Innovation happens in a particular area by a particular group, but that cannot flow or diffuse to the larger ecosystem. So open innovation is an idea that is particularly relevant in education where I can take someone's innovation and build on top. In the case of the generative AI example I gave, DIGSHA took generative AI technology, took AI for Bharat, open DPIs for language translation and added a new module on DIGSHA. This also means that other countries can take certain parts of Sunbird, combine it with other technologies and create their own unique DPIs. And open innovation, I believe, is a way to maximize the full power of DPIs. But like with anything at XSTEP, we believe all solutions have to be people plus technology. And in edtech, there is a danger of starting with technology and not with people, the learners, the teachers, the parents and the use cases. So to conclude, our learning has been start with where a system is. What are the current problems it is facing? What can be transformed through a combination of a digital infrastructure, innovation on top, in line with existing policies? And then work with the large existing ecosystem in your countries and avoid the pitfall of just looking at what is not there. Look at what you already have. In India, we had a lot of textbooks. In India, we knew teachers had cell phones. A lot of schools had basic infrastructure. Building on what is abundant to create your own DPI is very important. And resisting the urge to copy another DPI because it worked well. It's a fascinating journey. In India, we are still on the journey. But I would be very happy to learn from your experiences and share our experiences in more detail for those interested. Thank you.



> girl to be transformed into a confident teenager by the time she is complete with her school
> education. That 15 years is a complex set of actors, parents, teachers, administrators, content
> creators, academicians, community, technologists, whole lot of people come together, the classmates
> of that girl, all of them come together to make it an education journey for that 3-year-old to
> become an 18-year-old. In a country like that, we have 260 million such children learning in 22
> languages across 60 boards of education, each autonomous, taught in 1.4 million schools, taught by
> 9.5 million teachers. When you are faced with something as large, as diverse, as complex as this,
> the question we asked as AXSTEP Foundation when we were set up in 2015 was what is the role
> technology can play in solving for education outcomes. That thinking led us to the DPI approach and
> some of us were anyway part of the Aadhaar startup journey under the leadership of Nandan Nilakani,
> who is also my co-founder at AXSTEP Foundation with Rohini Nilakani, so we knew how DPIs work. The
> question was how could we use DPIs in education and if you think about it, the three key constructs
> of DPI, which is a strong digital backbone or infrastructure, appropriate policies and innovation
> by the ecosystem are even more relevant in education and that is how we conceived of a digital
> infrastructure for education. In fact, it is called DIGSHA, which is Digital Infrastructure for
> Knowledge Sharing. AXSTEP Foundation co-created it with the Government of India and many other
> players. It was launched in around December 2017 as offering access to local content. So what it
> did was when the Government of India offered this as an infrastructure to all those 60 boards, each
> board could create their own digital content and link it to the existing textbooks. What this did
> was it allowed children to access digital content relevant to them, relevant to the topic they are
> learning through the mechanism of unique QR codes in the textbook. So as a result, 600 million plus
> textbooks all over India became QR coded and connected to digital content, each created by their
> respective boards and as part of implementing the national education policy, where digital
> infrastructure is a crucial component of technology. What this did was over 17, 18, 19 and before
> the pandemic of 2020, there was a mass scale adoption. More than 30 states and union territories
> out of India's 36 came on board this digital infrastructure offered by the Government of India. So
> DIGSHA was a Government of India infrastructure, AXSTEP and some of the other not-for-profits came
> together and co-created this. This infrastructure on which the various other boards innovated on in
> their own language with their own content and in line with the broad policy architecture of the
> National Education Policy 2020 is again a very good example of digital public infrastructure in
> education. But what is DPI? It is that which offers a specific service at population scale so that
> others don't have to create that service. Because the Government of India provided this national
> infrastructure as a service, the various boards could now use that service to link their existing
> physical content in textbooks in their language with their curriculum to the relevant digital
> content, innovation on top and all in line with existing policies. So this was the first major use
> case of DPI and an example of innovation by the ecosystem on top. The second example of why DIGSHA
> is a good DPI is it is evolvable and I'll give you two examples of that. During pandemic, when
> school shut down, teacher training also shut down, learning by children continued because those
> textbooks were already QR coded and children could access that digital content sitting at home. But
> how do teachers come together and train? So very quickly another service was added on DIGSHA which
> is teacher training and because DIGSHA was designed as a DPI and using the building blocks, very
> rapidly a teacher training building block was added. As a result, over the next 2-3 years, 22,000
> courses have been created by the ecosystem and close to 140 million teacher credentials have been
> issued, around 7 million teachers undergoing these courses and getting the 140 million credentials.
> Another part of a good DPI is inclusive innovation. In DIGSHA, it did not matter what kind of a
> device you had, whether it was a mobile phone, a laptop, a tablet and if a child did not have
> access to a digital device, the teachers and most teachers in India have a device, the teachers
> could showcase that content in class either through televisions like in certain states or in some
> cases through their own personal mobile phones which has happened in other states. So thinking of
> inclusive innovation while creating a DPI was a necessity for a country as large and diverse as
> India. But that's not enough of a DPI because no DPI is born perfect and fully complete. A good DPI
> can and must keep evolving. So when the generative AI disruption hit us all in 2022, there was a
> great opportunity for DIGSHA to leverage that and add a generative AI component into DIGSHA. So
> very quickly, another service was added wherein a combination of generative AI on Indian languages
> and using open source digital building blocks came together to create a series of bots for
> teachers, for parents where they could access relevant information about how to teach better for
> teachers, about how to handle children, young children at home for parents and these answers are in
> the local language of the teacher or the parent and they refer to official documents which are
> mostly in English or Hindi so that the answers are authoritative and also relevant to the question.
> So this is a good example of the evolvability of a DPI and how DIGSHA was designed for
> evolvability. The other aspect of a DPI is does it allow the ecosystem to work better together. As
> I explained, education is a complex ecosystem, there are so many people working together. A good
> DPI should be able to help them work better and a way to do that is through responsible data
> sharing, data on usage of content, data on credentialing, which teachers have got which credentials
> on completing courses and DIGSHA was designed for sharing of data responsibly within the system by
> those who needed the data. It could be data about course completion, data about which content in
> which language of which subject was being used more so that the authorities can take better
> decisions on the back of that. In the case of DIGSHA, what was different was it was built on a set
> of open source code that AXSTEP put together what we call as digital public goods. In this case, it
> is Sunbird. What a well-designed digital public good does for DPIs is it allows the creation of
> multiple DPIs. So, while DIGSHA was created using Sunbird, another learning platform called IGOT
> was also created. This one by the Department of Personnel and Training and specifically for
> training government employees. Two different DPIs built of the same digital public good. Why is
> this important? When other countries want to create their own DPI in education, they do not need to
> start from scratch. They can take openly available free to use public good like Sunbird and create
> their own DIGSHA and there is a lot of interest and a lot of efforts going on to take Sunbird by
> other countries and build their own learning platforms, their own DPIs on top and that brings me
> back to a central concept that can differentiate a great DPI from a good DPI which is the idea of
> open innovation. In education in particular, we have seen islands of excellence. We have seen silos
> of innovation. Innovation happens in a particular area by a particular group but that cannot flow
> or diffuse to the larger ecosystem. So, open innovation is an idea that is particularly relevant in
> education where I can take someone's innovation and build on top. In the case of the generative AI
> example I gave, DIGSHA took generative AI technology, took AI for Bharat, open DPIs for language
> translation and added a new module on DIGSHA. This also means that other countries can take certain
> parts of Sunbird, combine it with other technologies and create their own unique DPIs and open
> innovation I believe is a way to maximize the full power of DPIs but like with anything at XSTEP,
> we believe all solutions have to be people plus technology and in edtech, there is a danger of
> starting with technology and not with people, the learners, the teachers, the parents and the use
> cases. So, to conclude, our learning has been start with where a system is, what are the current
> problems it is facing, what can be transformed through a combination of a digital infrastructure,
> innovation on top in line with existing policies and then work with the large existing ecosystem in
> your countries and avoid the pitfall of just looking at what is not there. Look at what you already
> have. In India, we had a lot of textbooks. In India, we knew teachers had cell phones. A lot of
> schools had basic infrastructure. Building on what is abundant to create your own DPI is very
> important and resisting the urge to copying another DPI because it worked well. It is a fascinating
> journey and in India, we are still on the journey but I would be very happy to learn from your
> experiences and share our experiences in more detail for those interested. Thank you. Thank you.






   6. DPI-As-A-Packaged-Solution \#DaaS in a nutshell | CDPI at GTS Innovation Dialogue 2024  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vDWHVlijDKM](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vDWHVlijDKM) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vDWHVlijDKM&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> Can I please bring on Kamiya Chandra, the Chief Strategy Officer at the Centre for Digital Public Infrastructure, who's been doing better, cheaper, faster. Kamiya, tell us how. Can you guys hear me clearly? So I think Pramod has told you kind of what the next six years, five to six years should look like. I want to talk you through what you can do in six weeks if you're working in a country. Because I think we have to span both of those horizons. We have to think six years, ten years ahead, and we have to think what can we get done to affect millions of lives in the next six weeks. So I'm Kamiya, I'm from the Centre for Digital Public Infrastructure, which Pramod is the co-chair of, and we sort of provide pro bono technology architecture advice. But this idea of DAS or DPI as a package solution, this thinking about how do we drive rapid deployment is actually not a CDPI effort. You see all of these logos, I think most of the logos on that screen, plus the ones on the top, have all come together in sort of an ecosystem approach, because DPIs are about ecosystems. They're not ever about just one logo. So what have we seen in countries? We've seen that the path to DPI implementation is full of potholes, right? You have issues around timelines, because of the timeline of procurement, the timeline to get funding approval, which I'm sure will be discussed in the breakout room. You have technology vendor issues and concerns, corrections, calibrations, change requests, famously, across the sector. You have hiring, you have capacity issues. All of these are major challenges that are preventing countries from saying, hey, this is a great idea. This is a great concept. We know it's been done before in so many countries around the world, from Brazil to Estonia to Singapore to India to Thailand and beyond. How do we get something like this for the baseline identities, payments, data sharing, that can actually get deployed in a short period of time? So the intent to implement is no longer in question. There's at least 35 countries that we're talking to personally, and between the World Bank with 50-plus active projects, UNDP with 40, 50 countries' active projects, it's very clear that the countries have intent. The question is now, how do we get to rapid, right? In order to get to rapid, we want you to think of two separate pieces. Let's see if we can separate the problem out. There's one side of the problem, which is sort of a greenfield implementation. You're starting from scratch, multimillion-dollar project. You have to enroll maybe 50 million people into a new system. You have to start a new payment system completely from scratch. Let's put those aside, because counterintuitively, most countries are not starting from scratch. Most countries already have digital assets that they actually can just build on. They already have some ID system that's functioning in the country. They have some payments infrastructure. They have some paper certificates as a method of data sharing, or even digital certificates, PDFs, that they're using to generate land records, or they're using to give you your social benefits, or they're using to register marriages and licenses, right, of all kinds. Our question is, if we focus on that right side, can we rapidly upgrade an existing digital infrastructure? Let's say you have an ID system. Let's add a digital authentication mechanism, a digital mechanism to share those profile data fields to anyone else in the ecosystem who can reuse it. Let's start with a digital credential or certificate that Pramod talked about and add a digitally signed QR code on it, so that paper that's so easy to travel across borders with becomes a digital public infrastructure for the person and a mechanism of data sharing. Can we unlock these kinds of rapid deployments? And our thinking here is that this rapid upgrade section, these can be prepackaged very well in order to avoid these risks, right? The risks of security issues, privacy vulnerabilities, the risks of vendor lock-in and vendor challenges, the risks of technology architecture errors in framing it, in order to minimize those risks and to drive speed and scale across the 50-plus countries that want to implement this in a rapid time, we think the prepackaging is key. Now, what do we mean when we say prepackaging? First is the technology part. What does that mean? It just means reusable code, right? It can be open source code. It can be even code share on a bilateral arrangement from one entity to another. But the code doesn't have to be recreated from scratch. It also means you can leverage, if you choose, public or private clouds, right? So hyperscalers in the room can be used for hosted solutions, but that's not mandated. You can also host it on premises if you have your own servers or if you have some form of private cloud. All three, when your technology hardware infrastructure is ready and the code is reusable, your tech can be prepackaged to minimize mistakes and errors. But the technology providers who have these packages can't scale themselves to 50 or 100 countries to the demand, because that's just too large an ask of them. So how do we get them to move faster? The next step is teams. They have to train private service provider teams to say, actually, this is how you deploy my package, right? So with an ecosystem of pre-trained private service providers, many of whom are in this room and have gone through the training, by the way, you can actually choose your army as a country, right? You can say I don't just want this prepackaged technology. I want a program team. I want that team to know how to deploy the specific building block in the DPI approach. It's been certified as a trained service provider on the prepackaged technology. But that team sometimes has to also be made sure that they're enabled with the right building block so that they don't make mistakes either, right? And for that, we created a bunch of standard artifacts, a standard playbook to, again, minimize the scope for mistakes to increase speed and to drive scale. This includes a standardized scope documentation, standardized framing of how big the team should be and what capacity role job titles should be on the team. It includes thinking around what the governance on the team should be. Who's in the steering committee? Who's in the program committee? How often should the program team meet versus the steering committee meet? A short policy paper template, a standard costing model. These kinds of artifacts are the things that allow those pre-trained teams to actually succeed. Fourth, the governance arrangements between all of the teams that interact with each other. Legal partnerships cannot be underestimated. So for this, we've put together sort of shared ecosystem participation terms that say all of the service providers in this ecosystem commit to these guidelines on cybersecurity, on individual privacy, on protecting the country's interests, on contributing back to the open source code in case there are changes that are made and so on to prevent technology risks from taking off. It also includes standardized bilateral agreements between a country and a technology provider, between the technology open reusable code provider and a private service provider and so on. So we minimize the time for the lawyers to get in the room and spend their weeks, right, and earn their share in that sense. And finally, funding. This is the piece of the puzzle where we're aiming for two weeks for a country approval. Our thinking is if you've gone through all of the four stages before that, we have prepackaged the tech, prepackaged the process, reduced the risks, as well as templatized the implementation, the funding approval doesn't have to be as long as it is per country, right? You do it at an international level, at a program level, and you say, all right, this country wants to deploy this standard product. This is not rocket science to me anymore. So you can fast track it. And thankfully, we've sort of with co-developed support raised about

   7. What is Digital Public Infrastructure?  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8CACZ4tCJWg](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8CACZ4tCJWg) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8CACZ4tCJWg&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> You've definitely heard of Digital Public Infrastructure, or DPI, but can you explain what it really is? Let's take a look at it through the eyes of billions of people at the risk of being left behind. Meet Amari. Amari needs to prove she owns her land, pay for government fees, and receive payments for various services. She needs to provide the Ministry of Lands a copy of her ID card, an authorization from her bank, and half a dozen other documents. Just imagine how much time she would save if she could access these documents with just a few clicks. The Ministry could then also quickly make sure her documents are authentic. For this to happen, identity, credentials, and payment services must be digitized and connected with each other, both across ministries and private data providers like banks. This is exactly what DPI is all about. By digitizing more public and private services on common infrastructure, we will create more value for each of us. To actually make Amari's life easier, some rules must be respected. Think of a railway system, with stations, tracks, and trains. Whatever the length of the train, the wheel spacing must be identical. Whatever route it takes, the signaling must be consistent. And whatever the train's schedule, time must be measured uniformly across stations. This guarantees the smooth functioning of the infrastructure and ensures scale-up from network effects by breaking down silos. The result? Travelers get to their destinations safely and comfortably. With built-in open standards that are reusable, not only does it become easier for Amari to access information and payments, but this infrastructure can connect everyone, everywhere, to other opportunities and services. With privacy embedded up front in the design, people can rest assured that their data will be kept safe and secure. The world over, countries are starting to build their own digital railways. The good news is they can pick among the best existing practices and adapt them to the tastes and travel habits of their people. The tracks we lay today will shape the infrastructure we will share tomorrow. So, let's join forces and shape this new infrastructure towards ensuring that DPI is safe, accessible, green, financed, and future-ready. For more UN videos visit www.un.org

> of their people. The tracks we lay today will shape the infrastructure we will share tomorrow. So,

> green, financed, and future-ready. For more UN videos visit www.un.org
