   9. DPI Masterclass – Mr. Rahul Matthan (Partner, Trilegal) | Governance frameworks to support DPI  
       [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aL7elJE4XQY](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aL7elJE4XQY) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aL7elJE4XQY&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> Gosh, I mean, I have been involved with DPI probably since the very beginning. I was working with Nandan on the sides on Aadhaar and around the right to privacy type of issues, worked a lot around the privacy principles that have to be built into an identity system and then most recently I got involved in DIPA, the Data Permanent Protection Architecture and that is a very privacy preserving type of architecture but it is a very different type of architecture and to explain that in the context of data protection laws as well as to explain to all the people who are building it, how to embed privacy data protection principles into the design of the infrastructure was where I was involved. Over the years in all the various DPI's, all these various issues that keep coming up around open markets, competition, open source software, then of course a lot of the contractual design and all of those sorts of things. So yeah, off and on maybe 15 years I have been hovering on the outskirts of the DPI ecosystem but very much working on the role of law and governance with DPI. The most important thing to understand when you are looking at governance for digital public infrastructure is that you have the traditional frameworks and the traditional frameworks are the waterfall as I see it is statutes and then contracts and statutes are broken up into constitutional provisions, then actual laws, delegated legislation which is regulations and once you have got all of that because governance is described by these things and this is all well before we get to contracts and then once you have sorted out the governance frameworks in this set of statutes and the set of documents, you then move to inter-party governance which is between the participants of the DPI ecosystem that is covered in contracts. But when you are thinking about DPI you have an additional way to look at this and that is that it is possible for you to build these legal principles directly into the code of the infrastructure and so really that is where I started from. I was like look we have got the laws, we have got the regulations, we have got the contracts but we can also actually embed these principles directly into the infrastructure and once you have that then I think it takes two levels of skill. One is to understand what the technology can do, what it is doing and then to understand how legal principles can actually be incorporated into the code and the design of the way the infrastructure is built. So I think that is where I started but as you get more and more into that you realize that look you can embed design legal principles into the design of DPI but you have to have some kind of contractual framework and then we rethought the way the contractual framework would be designed. These are all massive multi-party systems and so if you do traditional bi-partite contracts you will struggle and so we had to come up with this ecosystem participation terms concept where you have got this central pillar around which people sort of agree to participate based on their role. So it is a role based participation on a standard agreement depending on what role you have your obligations are inherited from the way in which that central pillar is designed. But even that is not enough. You need to have then actual institutional governance frameworks. So you need to have an institutional framework for who is going to decide what the protocols are going to be, who is going to decide what version two of the ecosystem participation terms are going to be and for that you need to have technical standards organizations which will determine what the protocols would be, what the next level of protocols would be and you need to have self-regulatory organizations which will within the sectoral ecosystem decide the actual way in which participation will take place. In many of these sectors you require certification and you require constant audits and you require various new features and new products that come out into the ecosystem. How do we ensure that they align with the legal principles? So that is the institutional governance design. So if I was just to sum up we have got the traditional provisions which is laws, regulations, contracts. You have got the ability to embed legal principles from laws, regulations, contracts directly into the code and then you have got governance of institutions that are required to maintain the code, that are required to evolve the code and also just maintain the ecosystem. And this is the full range of legal instruments that you have when you are working in this space. What is the point of building for the ecosystem? The point is that you are looking to build for the ecosystem. You are not building for an individual or for an individual entity. When you are building for an individual entity you will put in belts and braces. That is what we call it in legal terms. You will put in a clause and then you put in another clause even though that first clause dealt with everything you are like look I do not know maybe there is a need to put another clause in. And that is sort of the approach. Now when you are building for the ecosystem you are actually putting yourself in the shoes of all the participants of the ecosystem and you are trying to figure out what is in the interest of all these people. Now when you are thinking ecosystem you will realize that the belts and braces you would put in for just the one entity is actually coming in the way of the benefits to the other entity or the way in which the other entity will function. And when you realize that you start to strip down what your ask is because every asker link that you put in is something that you are detracting from someone else in the ecosystem in terms of what they can do or the mitigations they get etc. And therefore the only way to build ecosystem contracts where you are not the top, the head honcho of the ecosystem and everyone else is going to do what you say. You are literally negotiating this for the weakest chain in the link as well. In that type of environment you will find that you will actually build out the minimal, the most minimal contract. But I think it is one step further. I think when you build an end to end digital ecosystem a lot of what lawyers would traditionally put into a contract can actually be delivered through code. What do I mean by this? When we are engaging with each other as human beings, as persons, I cannot control how you perform. I can only impose an obligation on you under a contract. But if you are functioning in a digital ecosystem, the way the code operates is almost as good as a law. In which case I do not have to tell you to do A, B, C or I do not have to say that if I do A you will do B because the code is designed such that when it receives input A it will deliver output B. And I do not really now need to make this part of the contract because it will happen. So the next level of minimization that we took is we went through the workflows, the digital workflows and we eliminated from contractual obligations all those workflows that typically we would put into a contract but which are now being performed in code. And so the net result of these two actions was that we got a very minimal kind of a contract. And of course negotiating this is a challenge because the other side is so used to seeing the clauses in the contract that we had to tell them, okay, look you want the obligation, we will give you the obligation but we are actually giving the obligation in code. So you should not be negotiating liability, you should be testing whether the code actually performs as we promised it would perform. So it is a slight shift in the way in which the lawyers and the business people think. In other circumstances the business people are telling the lawyers, have you protected my downside. Now we are forcing the business people to tell the software developers, does the code perform as it is advertised. Because if it does then I do not have to insist that the lawyers put this in because it will perform as it is. So this is another shift in thinking that is only possible in these kinds of digital public infrastructure because it is at a sector wide level. Every entity in the sector that is performing this particular service is performing it on this digital infrastructure. And so we can make sure that the digital infrastructure itself is actually operating almost like a contract and ensuring that all the parties perform their contracts. And I will take it one step further because obviously if the digital infrastructure fails then some obligation will not be performed. If there is an outage and a server does not work and you have got a 9 layer set of events. At the 7th layer the server is not working and therefore the transaction does not complete. What we said was we will put in place dashboards. We will put in place red and green lights to say that if we started the transaction, if all the lights are green that means the transaction is through. If the light is red we will see how many of the 7 lights were green before it turned to red. And if the 5th light is red then we know that at that 5th transaction it failed. And so we have also got a very powerful dispute resolution mechanism to say that look it is your fault that the transaction did not go through because your server failed or because you know something happened at that place. And rather than put the blame on you know I do not know who, we will say look 7 people were supposed to do things. We did not know who did it. We now have to go to court and decide. We do not have to do any of that because the evidence is clear in the dashboard. So these are the features that you will get when you are trying to build governance mechanisms directly into code. The idea is in the governance and the legal frameworks for DPI we borrowed heavily from the actual principles of DPI. You know unbundling is an example. This minimization is essentially stripping out the things which are not important. That is a very DPI principle. We unbundle the DPI to its atomic element and then we put it together. We did the same thing with the contract. We stripped it down to its core elements and then we put it together. Modularity, we designed the legal framework such that there is an identity governance framework and that identity governance framework can be strapped on to a commerce framework such that you have an end-to-end solution for G2P payments where the identity piece and all the privacy protections around the identity piece is in that identity element. But that forms part of the entire G2P solution because the identity piece, the legal and the governance pieces around that is dealt with already in the system and we do not have to rewrite that again. So that whole reuse, modularity, all of those things are built in. And then federated data also keeping a lot of these systems federated such that we do not concentrate data into a single place. That is very much a data security obligation under various legal frameworks and that is how we implement that. So you are right. I think there are many borrowings that we have done from the way in which DPI as a technology has been built and the way in which the governance of DPI has been conceived of in this framework. I think we need to recognize that many things keep changing. None of these systems that we build are static and should not be static. I think a lot of the design of DPI is such that it can evolve. Evolvability is a core element of digital public infrastructure and evolvability therefore has to also be a core element of the governance of digital public infrastructure. So I had mentioned two institutions for governance, TSOs and SROs. TSOs are technical standards organizations. So to answer your question about new technologies, AI, but it could even be new products. We come into this financial services industry thinking that we want to give loans to people but actually we realize that we want to do wealth management for them. And so we need new products that are coming out and the protocols that we have written may be protocols for loans but we actually need protocols for wealth management. All of that will be dealt with by the technical standards organization which will say that look, now AI has come in and we need to make this available in multiple languages and rather than insist that it be translated in multiple languages, let us just make an API to plug a Bhashani type language in or some language translation in such that whichever country we are in the world, we can translate it to whichever language is relevant. That will be done by the technical standards organization because it may require a modification of the existing protocol to incorporate a new standard, a new technical standard. And then you have got the contracts, the legal frameworks, you have got certification and audit, various of these sorts of things that in a particular sector will be surfaced by a self-regulating organization. So an SRO or a self-regulating organization in a given sector is an entity or an institution that understands that space very well. So let us say in the financial services sector, you will have an SRO in the financial services sector to say look, when we are talking about data sharing or data exchange in the financial services sector, these are the problems that are coming up. People are saying that I need this additional new product or what you have built me works really well for loans but it is not working so well for wealth management, there are these additional features that I need. And then we will say that okay, now in order to put these additional features, we need some regulations or we need some additional certification. And then the SRO will come up and say okay, we need these amendments to be done from the legal side, the contractual side, the regulatory side and perhaps even the statutory side. And obviously the SRO cannot amend the laws that has to be done by parliament but the SRO can certainly say that on the ground we are finding these problems with the way the law works and so can we recommend this change. So these are the two ways in which I think is the most practical way in which you evolve. I think if you wait for legislators to make the change, they will only make the change once there is enough complaints in the system that this is not working, that is probably too late. Having SROs on the ground and TSOs on the ground are people who are actually implementing these DPIs and they are finding that there are some actual problems on the ground as we are implementing it and they can surface it up much faster. So this would be a way to actually evolve the legal frameworks at the same pace as DPI will evolve the technical frameworks. Extremely challenging, as you are saying, lawyers are only used to dealing with contracts and to convince them that this would perform in code was extremely challenging. Of course I had an ally in the fact that the first person I had to convince was Pramod. Pramod Rao was at that point in time the general counsel of ICICI bank and I spent a lot of time having this conversation with him. I wouldn't say a lot of time, compared to the total amount of time we spent trying to put this ecosystem together, it was actually quite easy to speak with Pramod and get him to understand. But once I could get him to understand, in a sense he was my ally and he would, having understood the power and the potential of this and in his own ecosystem realized that actually this will work, it was then much easier because we have got a general counsel of a big bank, well respected in the banking sector who could then go and in their own language convince other general counsels of other banks to say, look this is how we think it will work, we are comfortable with this, I think you can also get comfortable. And then of course there is a bit of a momentum with this, once 3-4 banks say yes it's okay and it's also when the transactions are working and you are finding that we really don't need these clauses, the code is performing as advertised, it becomes easier. So my experience with this is you have to put in a lot of effort to try and change the path the first time and with some time and as long as the system is effective, see the moment the system doesn't work then of course everything comes crashing down, the pendulum is going in the completely opposite direction. But so long as the system works as advertised and as more and more people are comfortable and convinced of it, then it works. When a country is looking to build a legal framework for governance, I think the instinctive response for any government is to try and see if I can build it into a law or regulation or something like that. My only advice to them is that you have another tool and that is to actually embed these legal principles directly into the infrastructure. You have the power to do it because you are the one who is signing off on the protocols that are being performed and you can insist that the protocols have certain legal functions. Now the power of this is that as much as you think your laws are powerful, your laws have to be enforced and as much as you've written it in the statute book, people can violate it until they get caught. The advantage of writing it into code is you cannot violate it at all because the code only performs in a binary way, either yes or no. And that is the power of instead of trying to put it into law that a hundred people will violate or maybe a hundred thousand people will violate before you actually get it to the court to adjudicate on. You can put it into the infrastructure so that no one can violate it. Now that is the power of understanding this approach and so I strongly urge countries that are looking to do this to actually first articulate what are the legal principles that they want to enshrine into their digital public infrastructure. It could be a combination of I want to have free markets, I want to ensure no monopolies can take place, I want to ensure that everyone has autonomy, I want to ensure that no data is taken without consent. Or it could be any slight subset of this. It could be in certain circumstances I want data to be sent because this is for the good of the country. Whatever it is, you have to decide up front these are the principles that I want to govern this particular sector. Some of those principles you have to enshrine into law and I think almost all the principles have to derive its sustenance from the constitution or some overarching legal framework that the country is bound by. But a lot of them instead of putting it into delegated legislation can actually be put into the code and the design of the infrastructure. And if you can do that you will get everyone participating on the infrastructure both the private sector parties, the government entities, everyone participating in that infrastructure will just by the act of participating in that infrastructure comply with the legal principles and the laws and the frameworks. And that is an extremely powerful thing for a country to have.

> My name is Nikhil, I am one of the co-founders of Setu. At Setu our mission is to make formal financial services affordable and accessible to everybody in India. So we are essentially an API infrastructure company. What this means is if any developer or any institution wants to build, digitize a part of their financial services, whether it could be on payments, whether it could be in KYC or whether it's underwriting, we provide API infrastructure which is built on top of the digital public infrastructure that's available in India. The major goal of digital public infrastructure in a country like India is to broadly we can classify it to two large buckets. One is to bring down the costs of transactions. This could be through payments infrastructure, this could be through KYC infrastructure and so on and so forth. And then the second is to also bring down the cost of onboarding a customer which means you know accessing different data, making sure underwriting happens and so on. So in India when we think about making formal financial services affordable and accessible to everybody, one of the major important levers for us to think about in this century at this point in time is how do we use digital, how do we use the internet, how do we use technology to bridge the gap that exists between what people want and what institutions are able to serve. So essentially DPI brings down the cost of delivering these digital financial services. Now when the cost of delivering these services come down, what this means is now you will be able to serve a large set of customers who you were not able to serve before. For example, let's say today if you go to a bank and if you apply for a loan at a branch, the cost of processing that loan is anywhere around 5000 rupees or broadly say 80 dollars. So you can't be spending 80 to 100 dollars as a cost of processing to give a 80 to 100 dollar loan, right, this economics never works. So the only way you can think about serving that particular customer base is by reducing your cost of processing and the cost of processing can be significantly reduced by using digital public infrastructure. So what does this mean? For example, the cost that would take for us to process an application offline at a branch which could be anywhere between 80 to 100 dollars can be brought down to a dollar by using digital public infrastructure. So when your cost becomes a dollar to process and you know apply application of a loan, you can now think about building sachet size products which could be for 100 dollar loans, could be even 50 dollar loans, it could be small insurances that people would want to buy on a daily basis, do savings, all of that is now possible which is why digital public infrastructure is very very important in a developing market like India. It brings down the cost of servicing customers and when that happens you will now be able to service a large set of customers and that's what our mission is. Let me take one example of a very good implementation of a DPI which is not so well known. So in India we have a bill payment system which is interoperable, open and accessible to everybody called the Bharat Bill Payment System. When it originally was designed it was thought about enabling digitization of bill payments which could be for utility payments like electricity, water, broadband, gas etc. What this meant is any consumer who is consuming these services from the government or even from a private agency need not go to the office of this particular service provider to pay their bills on a monthly basis but they could sit at home, use one of their favorite payment applications and complete a payment. But if you really look at the impact that it's having, how it's evolved is BBPS, the Bharat Bill Payment System also allows lenders or you know banks which are lending to customers to become a merchant or a biller on the system. So what this means is a consumer is now able to sit at home and also repay their loan without actually an agent needing to visit them, without actually a phone call and so on. So what this has done is for a lot of microfinance institutions, for a lot of NBFCs, for a lot of banks which serve in rural areas and underserved areas, it's brought down the cost of collections because just the mere act of sending somebody to your doorstep to collect cash and take that cash and go put it into let's say a nearest branch and then converting that into digital to you know if you look at the supply chain of the money, it's very expensive to do this. However in digital, the fact that the customer is sitting at home making a payment two clicks and the money reaches much faster, it's more efficient, it's super low cost, this is basically brought down the cost of collections for a lender. Now when you bring down the cost of collections for a lender, if you look at any lending industry, any lending customer, one of the major expenses that they have you know when they lend to customers is the cost of collections. So when you are able to reduce the cost of collections, you are reducing the cost of operations of a lender and as a result of that if the cost of operations come down, this means that the end borrower can actually get the loan at a lower cost. So this is how DPI is impacting at various different levels. So we might see the first order effect that it's low cost collection, but the longer term second order effect of this is that because the cost of collections has come down, the cost of operations come down and as a result of that the cost of borrowing comes down for the end borrower. So this is a good example of how DPI is being used in the digital financial services space. Generally, you know when fintechs and payment infrastructure or infrastructure companies have been built, you know you can think about companies being built on or companies building closed loop systems where you control the demand and the supply side and then you build value added services, products etc. Now one of the big problems of this is you are always limited to the supply and demand that you can build and you know it has its pros and cons. It has pros because it has bigger margins, but it has cons because you don't have a large base so you won't have large impact and so on. And then the other approach is to adapt to open loop technologies and then build on top of it. So when we were starting, we were very clear that rather than fighting DPI or open loop, we will completely embrace it and become an innovation layer on top of it because we genuinely believe over the long term consumers would love choice, interoperability will be more important and consumers seek this and it's also very good for the businesses which are interacting with these DPI's because at the end of the day they get cost benefits etc. So our major decision fork in our process of building our company and the startup was you know do we try and go the closed loop route or do we completely embrace open loop and we chose the latter and that was probably one of the major design decisions that we had to take when we started the company. So the good news about working on open loop is that it's just not you, it's actually being a part of the ecosystem. To me DPI in India has become more like a movement which has various different people right from banks who have been there for a long time to new innovators like fintechs which have come up to regulators, the government and everybody basically playing a role in coming together to make each of these DPI work and as a result of that you know as they say a rising tide takes everybody along, it's been one such journey for us right. So as far as the ecosystem is winning we will continue to win and we have seen tremendous progress we've made across payments, KYC and data infrastructure building on top of the digital public infra available in India. There are generally a lot of challenges when a DPI comes to life especially in a country like India where you have so many different stakeholders, various different people participating in it. The challenges start from you know what should be the standard operating procedures, how do we ensure data security, pricing and commercials, uptimes and SLAs and so on. Because one of the things we need to understand about DPI is you know you kind of give away some part of the process or control to other systems which are not under your control and this is really hard to do for many institutions because generally our mental model is the more you are in control the more efficient it is the better the results are. It takes some time for people to move from that to see benefits but because DPI has this beautiful innate virtue of creating network effects it always you know trumps over you know what an individual can do. Like they say you know the sum of parts is always more powerful than the part itself and it's one such thing and generally I think because of the experience of working and building and adapting multiple DPI's we've now created a kind of a playbook of what it takes for it to come to life, what kind of institutions are required to solve problems, what kind of governance frameworks are required, what kind of regulatory and legal framework is required to make it work. So that way I think you know as you start implementing and start using DPI you end up building some sort of a mental model and framework to solve these problems. Most of these problems are you know solved generally by keeping the individual's interest but rather thinking about the collective benefit all of us would get. This is little hard to do especially when companies are chasing profits but I think if we are all you know if we are committed to this problem if we all put our heads together because the opportunity is so large, the problems are so large, the impact is so large that no one institution or one individual can solve this. It takes the collective to come together and I think that's worked out very well for us in the India context. I do think that DPI is a level playing field. I would like to make a counter argument or this thing that typically our mental model says that okay you have incumbents and then you have you know innovators and the innovators come and eat incumbents breakfast or lunch but I actually think it also holds true in the other way around. If you are actually empathetic to incumbents so let's take a bank for example which has been around for let's say 50 years or 100 years and I have been doing things the same way. It is really hard for a large organization to actually react to this and one of the reasons why innovators win is because their reaction time is very less. It's not like anyone's born with some special superpowers and so on but if you really cut down to the brass tacks it's that the fintechs or any new company is small in nature so decisions are made faster, your risk taking appetite is higher etc. etc. and so on and that typically gives them an edge and obviously one of the biggest beneficiaries of DPI is fintechs. They leverage it and so on but I do think the other side of it is like because the DPI exists and as a result of this there is some sort of a design language that's created that this is how a customer's onboarding would happen, this is how a customer's bill payment would happen or this is how a customer's KYC would happen. Now that started resulting in the banks and others also saying hey okay now that the regulator has blessed this, now that the government legal law enables us to do so, let's also follow these principles and it's cut down their time to actually make these decisions, get on top of it, leverage it and you know and challenge the innovators as well. So in some sense you know the level playing field kind of goes both ways where you know it does provide an opportunity for anybody with irrespective of how big or small they are to leverage it and use it to its best so that there's actual positive impact on the people that they're trying to serve. So that's how I would probably you know put this together that I don't think one loses or the other wins in this. It does create a level playing field where everyone has equal rights and you know right to win in this market. UPI is a beautiful example of this ecosystem coming together where banks play a very very important role as the payment service provider. Banks also play a very very important role as the custodian of the money of the customers but this they were also the enabler of you know FinTechs which are built on top of the payment service providers which you know could be the examples of phone pay, Google Pay, Paytm, WhatsApp and many others who have innovated on top of this layer and this has enabled the banks to continue to do what they do best which is essentially keep the deposits, the trust of their customers, do what they are best at in banking to bring in deposits, lend it to their customers and so on but at the same time enable superior user experiences on payments by creating this beautiful ecosystem of third party application providers, different FinTechs on merchant solutions etc. Where this lovely Jugalbandi, we use this word called Jugalbandi which is like a loosely used word to something like of a jazz band in India where everyone's got their instruments and they're all playing in harmony and they're in a flow state. It's a very good example of everybody coming together and the incumbents actually partnering with FinTechs or innovators and shaping disruption. I do think that you know there's a lot of unnecessary focus spent on people talking about profit pools in the DPI itself. The fact that it's called digital public infrastructure is to say that this fundamental infrastructure is like a public good. By definition, by design, it's meant to help the society at large to solve large problems like for example in our daily lives it could be roads, it could be bridges, it could be metro, train tickets etc. You can't expect it to be super profit making and so on because these are fundamental utilities that are necessary and DPI is basically nothing but the digital version of these infrastructure or public goods. So I think that we need to clearly understand that that's what the role and the definition of that part is and for everything else like how roads created commerce because businesses started interacting with each other and because as a result of that commerce got built. Similarly, you have internet and you have like all these platforms on top of it like Google and Facebook and many other companies or Amazon that got built on top of this. They created tremendous value. So I think it's important to understand that DPI is a value creation layer but not a value capture layer. So DPI enables value creation but it's not the layer in which you capture value. So it's very important to have this mental model that it's an enabling infrastructure and then your job as an entrepreneur or as an innovator or someone working in a large company is to actually think about how do I best leverage this DPI to build new businesses, new products, new services where I can capture value. For anybody who's thinking of building on top of DPI, there are probably two broad advices that I would give them. One is to embrace DPI and not fight it because it's one of those fundamentally important things from a principles perspective. I do believe that the principles that DPI stands for will always trump profits and arbitrage etc. etc. and so on because the principles of interoperability, being open, being extensible, they're like little bit of like physics of sorts, laws of physics. It's so fundamental that it will always trump over, if you're thinking about it from a business law of physics perspective, I think it is always fair to assume that this will trump over whatever that you might think. So my first advice would be to embrace it and not fight it, whether you're an incumbent or whether you're a newcomer, I think it's important to embrace it. The second advice that I have is a lot of people might feel that hey by building the DPI, you've taken away profit pools. So what do I do now? How do I make money? Where do I add value and so on and so forth? I think it's very important to understand that as time goes by, there are a few technologies, there are a few things which will become bare necessary. For example, at some point in time, water might have been a very, or it's becoming a scarce resource, but then there's so many applications as a result that it's available. A more recent one that we all experience is the internet. So one can argue and say, hey, why don't we pay high prices for internet? It had so much value, etc. But they're all enabling layers. So we tend to typically forget the value that gets created on top of this fundamental infrastructure and focus too much on the fundamental infrastructure itself. So my advice to people would be to think about how do you add value on top of this layer irrespective of whichever area that you're working on, because DPI needs to be thought about as a building block that's available to you, which makes it easy, which makes it accessible for you to build on top of. But it's not the end of what you need to build. It's the beginning. It's the first brick that you're going to probably lay when you're building your company or your product or service. And then you really need to think about what are the value added layers that I can do on top of it, which is adding value to my customers, where I can capture value and build a profitable business. So these would be my two things. One is embrace and second is innovate on value creation on top of the DPI.








   11. DPI Masterclass – Dr. Pramod Varma (Chief architect of the India Stack) | DPI Masterclass  
        [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fc2TjULsSIc](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fc2TjULsSIc) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fc2TjULsSIc&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> So, when we think about what makes DPI scale, just before, it's not a technology scale per se, okay, it's important. DPI scale comes from its design, it's a way of thinking, it's a way of rolling it out, a way of getting it adopted. Many of those go into the scaling aspect of DPI. But before that, let's quickly check, just make sure that we are on the same plane in terms of what is DPI versus traditional digitization. There's a lot of confusion, people say everything is DPI, solutions are DPI, or digitization is DPI. It's okay, because it's just a term, not a big deal, but I think the real question for us is to, for us, we nuance it a little more. Nuance it in such a way that digitization is the way of bringing, you know, digital technologies to solve a particular thing. Digitization is a superset. So you might digitize citizen governance, you might digitize healthcare systems, you might digitize payment systems and other systems, you might digitize a banking system. You can digitize everything, because computers can be brought in. But DPI is a subset. That's important to understand. DPI differs from traditional digitization in precisely two ways. One, DPI is not the whole solution. DPI is one component of many, many solutions that will come about later. So essence of DPI is reuse of that component. So you have to create something that is reusable. In the physical world, you would say a tire in a car is a component. It's not a car by itself, you know, it's just a tire. Or, you know, a steering wheel is just a component. By itself, you can't do anything with it. You need the whole car. But these components make the assembly of the car faster, cheaper, when you make these components independently. So DPI is the same thinking in the digital world, where you create reusable components or services that are minimal in nature, but definitely focusing on the reuse aspect of it. And the second part of the DPI thinking is that who reuses them? We must ensure that when you create a reusable building block or a component, it's reusable by the ecosystem, private and public ecosystem. So both private and public or public and private ecosystems must be able to leverage this DPI building block as they build additional layers of solutions. And this is why things like identity, things like just moving money, payment, or components like GTP mapper for government to person payment, credentialing as an architecture infrastructure that you can do, by itself doesn't solve anything. It's just one component in the overall digitization journey that you have to go through. But digitization without DPI means everybody will rebuild this component again and again and again. So you have to make this generic components or building blocks reusable and independent of the solution. If you can do that, you have achieved the biggest transformation that you would see that you would start having the GPS effect. GPS was a reusable building block. All it said is where you are. And then you could combine that with smartphone, internet connectivity, and reimagine Uber, something like that. And ride hailing was reimagined. So very, very powerful possibility exists if you can re-internalize this idea that if we are able to create reusable building block, reusable by private and public ecosystems, then many, many subsequent solution get faster and cheaper. So this is very important starting point. Now when we build the DPI itself, now that we have understood it is just a component, it's just a building block, it's not the whole thing, we have to think about how do we design that one component. That's where the design principles come into play. Minimalism very, very key. Maximalism is not what you're looking at. Minimalism is what you're looking at. Reusability, that means it has to be designed as a context invariant or context generalized, context invariant block. You should not assume, oh, ID is going to be used for bank account opening, and hence ID system has some bank knowledge. Then how will it be used for health care? How will it be used for something else? How will it be used by private parties for maybe a airport check-in or a hotel check-in? So that means ID system should not be aware of the context in which ID is used. That means context invariant or context free building blocks have to be designed. So first one is minimalism. Second thing is context free, generalized design. And third is to design as a reusable through policies. So you have to create policies and technology readiness in terms of application programming interface and all APIs and all that thing so that it gets reusable. Whether it's online reusable, offline reusable, you can look at all those scenarios and make sure there's a policy and technology readiness for reusability as well. That's primarily what you do. If every component is built this way, minimal, generic, reusable, then we are suddenly, country will have a basket full of reusable component that now a solution builder can assemble faster and cheaper to build solutions. That is the essence of scaling. What do you mean by scaling? It's not just technology scaling, right? It is the program scaling, scaling of the DPI adoption, usage. So issuing identity is only one part. If identity is not used by banking system, healthcare systems, you know, benefits delivery systems and private innovations and public innovation, job, employment systems, if they're not used, why did you issue the ID, right? So it is important to think about designing scale into the DPI, not as a technology scale, the scale of the whole system. That means why would it be adopted? What is the incentive structure for adoption? What is the policy unblocking for adoption? What is the technology barriers for adoption, cost barriers for adoption? You have to ask these questions beyond the pure technology scale. Of course, if you put out an API, application programming interface on a cloud service, private or public cloud, obviously technology has to scale. But that's only a small portion. Technology is only a small portion of DPI scaling. DPI scaling is primarily because you cracked the incentive structure for individuals and entities, private public entities in the country. If you can crack that incentive structure, support through policy to remove friction, support through technology friction through capacity building and funding and all that thing to remove that friction. So remove the friction for adoption, crack the incentive, two things. Crack the incentive so that they know why should I use it otherwise. There's no incentive. Then why would anybody use it? So crack incentives for people and systems and remove friction for adoption. Once the incentive may be high, demand may be very high, but friction is very high. Then demand is high, but friction is high and it's not taking off. Or demand is not there. Then this won't take off. So cracking that incentive, demand structure is very key and removing the friction barrier. Friction comes primarily from policy friction, technology friction and cost, time friction, capacity friction. You have to analyze that and make sure that's removed. Scaling is precisely that. So DPI, most of the people think DPI is about tech. DPI has absolutely very little to do with tech. DPI is a way of thinking. It's a way of thinking in first to generate the component, building block, create a reusable, context-free building block. Then making sure there is incentive alignment for everyone to adopt it and removing the friction for adoption. If you do this, DPIs will scale. This is why it is also important to understand a copy-paste of a DPI may not work. Same DPI in one country might be high value. Demand may be very high. Value to the society may be high. Friction may be low. But in other society, like identity, in a developed country, nation, where the civil registries and existing IDs already exist, one more ID may not add any more value. So you have to really ask this question. Is there societal value, high value and low friction? You have to get into that quadrant, high value, low friction quadrant, and make sure your DPIs that you are rolling out falls into that high value, low friction quadrant. Then you build it generic, build it reusable, and remove the barriers, policy and technology and cost barrier for adoption. That's what. And very little to do with tech. So it's a way of thinking. I think it's much to do with thinking about scale. And I'll end with this, saying many people make mistake of thinking I will do an experiment in a highly controlled, small geographic area and hope that suddenly it will work for a billion people. What works at scale is different from scaling what works. And what works at scale comes from the original design. The design itself should work. You should think about scale. And you have to simulate in your mind what could go wrong, what could go wrong, why at scale anything that can go wrong, and design some of that into the design of the program or design of the tech in the DPI. If you don't design what works at scale, you cannot take a proof of concept and try scaling. It's very different. And that is something which I'll end with. So think scale from day one. Scale of incentives, scale of ecosystem, scale of use cases, scale of everything. Think everything from scale perspective.

   17. The Nigeria Digital Identity DPI Story  
        [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eM6xw5gXBs8](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eM6xw5gXBs8) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eM6xw5gXBs8&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> Good afternoon, my name is Engineer Alain Reussof, and I'm the Director of Software and Biometrics at the National Identity Management Commission, which is a government ID solution provider in Nigeria. The history of ID has taken a very long time in Nigeria. Most countries have the challenge of identifying their citizens. So in Nigeria, we had the first well-known ID project, which is in 2003. This was an out-of-the-box ID project, which was concessioned to a French company called Sagem, and they were able to capture about 56 million Nigerians at that time, and they issued an identity token. Unfortunately, after that program, the data, because it's a proprietary solution, the government at that time could not use the data, don't have access to the data. So that led to an enactment of an act of creating the National Identity Management Commission in 2007. With that act, in collaboration with the Department of Civic Registration at that time, created the NIMC, and we started the project afresh. And that's the project that we started, whereby the project was conceived so that there would be an enrollment solution, and you capture Nigerians, issue an 11-digit identification number, also issue a token, which is the card, a multipurpose card, and as well get involved with harmonization and integration with other identity agencies, where in this case, driver's license, the National Population Commission that is responsible for birth and death, and also the Nigerian Immigration Service that is responsible for issuing passports. Also, in addition to capturing, one of the roles is that you must capture the 10-fingerprint biometrics, and also capture the picture, which is face. Then the third modality, which is IRIS, we've not included IRIS, but there's a working plan to do an upgrade, to include IRIS as the third modality of biometric identification. Yeah, the lesson learned from the fallout from the Initial Identity Project is that the introduction of the local content. In this project, the software, the enrollment software, was designed and developed by local vendor. We have also, as a commission, developed an in-house enrollment application. Another layer that has happened in this case is the introduction of verification. What is the use of having an identity if you cannot verify? There's digital verification, whereby real-time verification, and then verification also with provisions for API, whereby other stakeholders, in this case government agencies, and also the private individuals to plug in. That has seen a lot of interest. We've been able to plug in the financial institutions, the banks, for luminary verification. By rule now in Nigeria, you cannot procure a passport or renew an existing passport without having the national identification number. So the NIN is embedded and coded on the passport. We are working with the Vehicle Registration Authority, whereby driver's license also, you will need to have an NIN to get your license. Also, we have PENCOM, which is the pension commission, whereby also to get those benefits, most of these government services, you need to have an NIN. What is the use of NINs? For example, one of the key private shareholders that are consuming the verification service are the telcos, the mobile telephone companies. It is by law now that for you to have a SIM card, you must have an NIN. Also, we've seen other use cases whereby employers of labor, both private and government, are requesting for NIN. We're also moving towards probably the airlines before they sell you a ticket or before you board a plane or get some services like hospitals, health care insurance. You need an NIN. So it's becoming viral and important to some of the services, both government and private, that citizens and legal residents consume. What are the challenges of the NIN? Absolutely, there are challenges. We've been able to... We are managing the challenges. There are still challenges, but they've reduced significantly. One of the major challenges is acceptance. Because of the level of literacy, most people still don't understand the idea that why would they need an identity. Nigeria is one of the few countries in the world where you can travel from north to south and nobody asks you for any means of identification. Few years ago, you can board a plane or enter public transport without identification, but that is changing now. That tells you the level of awareness. We also have... In that case, up to 2015, we had only about 7 million enrolments, but we were able to ramp up enrolments because of collaboration. One of the major collaborators that really advanced our cause are the banking system, which is the Nigeria Interbanking System. We harmonised about 15 million records of the BBN. We adopted those that meet the criteria. We were able to bring them on board and generated an NIN for them. Right now, we have about 103 or 104 million records in the database. It is projected that the estimated population of Nigeria is between 200 and 230. At this point, we can say that we have captured about 50% of the population. We are making much advancement and the awareness is becoming more now because of some of the key services that you cannot consume those services without having an NIN. We do believe that NIN has come to stay. Also, to make things easier in terms of verification, we have introduced digital ID. We have moved from issuing a piece of paper to having a digital copy on your phone or your smartphone. This is just to make life easier for the citizens and some of the benefits of what the National Identification System provides. We are also aware that a lot of citizens are unbanked because some of them live in areas where there is poor communication in the rural area. We have tried to make things easier for them. We partner with, we give licenses to private companies. We call them the front-end partners, whereby they can go to the interlands to places where they have to get areas to capture citizens. We have seen a lot of interest in that. Community leaders have been, public awareness has been made known on TV, on radio, on jingles, to make sure that every community is aware of the importance of the National Identification Number. Also, recently, there will be government programs like palliatives that the poorest of the poor will enjoy. One of the criteria is that for you to qualify for this program or for you to get this benefit, you must have your NIN. So we do believe that this will also increase adoption. We bring more people who naturally wouldn't have come for the service, will come to get registered for this NIN. Our next step is right now, the World Bank is already also picking trade. There's a program that's supporting Nigeria with a grant, whereby it is believed that within three years, we're supposed to be able to capture every Nigerian. So usually funding has been the challenge, but with this relationship with the World Bank, I think we will get some funds that will assist in wrapping up or concluding the enrollment. But identity is an ongoing process. We cannot capture everybody, because the projection in Nigeria is that newborns, we have about 7 million, 7 to 8 newborns every year. So you have 7 to 8 million people to capture yearly. And also modifications, people change names, people change addresses. So these are also services that you need to update your data, which we increase by the day, and the National Identity Management Commission will be on their toes to make sure they are ready and able to provide these services. So again, cross-border relationship, without standards, it will be difficult for you to move from one country to another. So one of the main reasons why we're here in India, in Bangalore especially, is to work with agencies to adopt the MOSIP program, whereby it is developed based on open source so that we can increase efficiency and be able to bring services to different stakeholders due to interoperability so that the foundational identity can drive the economy, both in financial and all other sectors of the economy. Well, they need to be proactive and know that it's a big project. The same way Nigeria, despite the fact that we've covered about 50% of the population, we're here in India to see how to learn from what India has done, to have 1.3 billion citizens and about 90% captured into the database is something to cherish. So we're here to learn and to adopt best practices. So any other country that also would like to start an identity program, you look at where it's been done before. There is Pakistan, there is India, there is now Nigeria, and there are a few countries also in Africa that have adopted best practices and came up with identity program that works for their community, but most importantly, based on well-known and acceptable standards so that there can be interoperability. So thank you again for the opportunity and thanks to our new Director General, Engineer Abisola Koka Odusote, who is now the new champion of identity. We're here to look at best ways of doing things, looking at best practices and also incorporating open source to make sure that what we have is sustainable and also to partner with other stakeholders, especially the financial sector, to make sure that all the services that they provide for the citizens are done in real time and to the benefit of the country. Thank you so much.

Some of the countries we speak to are well advanced in their open banking implementation journey and already have in place infrastructure which allows providers to access transaction data from banks. Other jurisdictions are just beginning this journey, taking their first steps towards conceptualization and design of open banking frameworks which will work for them. And of course, there are many places which are somewhere in between these two points. While open banking covers both payments and data sharing, open finance extends data sharing to other non-banking actors in the financial ecosystem, such as insurance, securities, and pensions, providing access to consumers' entire financial footprint. Open finance is an exciting and ambitious area and many financial authorities are considering their role in its development. It is clear, however, that open finance as a broader data sharing initiative will require coordination and consistency across stakeholders and sectors. Digital public infrastructure is the rail upon which private and public sector actors can innovate and build new applications and products that benefit consumers, including those who are currently underserved. Open banking and open finance rails are examples of this DPI approach and this is why I'm incredibly excited about today's session. By sharing the successful approaches to open finance among our regulatory community here today, we can learn the lessons of others, consider these insights in our own open finance implementation journeys. I think we strongly believe that financial inclusion at scale can be achieved in a country's lifetime. It may sound like an ambitious statement, but in just a few years, with a few targeted actions, we believe that it's truly possible to uplift millions into the formal financial ecosystem. And we believe this happens in three phases. First, we think it's important to enable people to access the formal financial system, those who don't even have accounts. And that first formal account is typically done through the digital public infrastructure of a low cost electronic KYC. If you're from a country where formal financial accounts are ubiquitous, nearly every adult is banked, then this may not be a concern for you. If you're from an emerging economy, where you maybe have some challenges with an unbanked population, this is an important precursor to first bring in people into accounts that can be used to transact with payments and data sharing. Then you get into the second phase of what we call engagement, which is the payments layer, which allows for people to actually use those newly created accounts to actually transact in their everyday lives. The important thing in building this kind of infrastructure is to make sure that it works for low value and high volumes of payments. So that means the street vendor on a road that is selling their merchandise for an extremely low value should be able to accessibly access digital payments. And that happens when digital payments is inclusive, it's interoperable and instant. Usually we focus only on the instant through real time payments. And that's where open banking or open finance for payments comes in as an approach beyond other approaches to digital payments. Finally, the third phase is to empower people with actually access to the financial services that will allow them to uplift their lives, whether that's a loan, an insurance product, or an investment that gives them some level of returns or security in their old age through a pension. These financial products counterintuitively depend on data sharing without the ability to share information at low cost, in a tamper proof manner, through individual consent, you cannot systematically scale financial inclusion of products because at the end of the day, an institution offering a financial product has a cost of doing so and has a revenue return. And unless we build the digital public infrastructure rails that we call financial data sharing open finance, that element will never take off on the on the credit side. And we've seen the this is the key reason why many emerging economies around the world are actually choosing to build open finance rails to drive access to credit and other financial products. We actually believe that financial inclusion via open finance is not just important for an economic growth or second order economic effects perspective, but actually for financial stability, which is core to any regulator. First, it actually allows you to strengthen AML supervision, when you have fewer dark transactions or an informal economy, terror financing and money laundering is easier to monitor when your transactions are actually being logged in financial statements. Second, it allows you to have more effective monetary policy permeation through your society, if most of the credit that's going that's being utilized by people is actually coming from formal institutions, rather from informal institutions, but how once you decide that you really want open finance in your country, how do you build this in a manner that will actually drive scale and security in your ecosystem, we believe that it's a techno legal approach that's really critical. What do we mean by this, it means a combination of a regulatory framework, and the right technology standards and specifications without which you can't scale. Let's take an example first of the regulatory framework. In the space of payments, clearly identifying and demarcating roles of all the actors is a critical next step to be able to driving, allowing each of them to do what they do best, allowing regulators to regulate and innovators to innovate. Fintechs and new sort of smaller mobile wallets are coming into the space, flooding into the space. The question is how can we prescribe their role in a way where they focus on user interface innovation, and not so much on the movement of money where there are higher risks. And so we see often that both Brazil and India have actually crafted a separate license in Brazil, the PISP license, and in India, the TPAP third party application provider license in payments for fintech players that can actually just work on the user experience of money because they can be close to users, they can onboard users, without actually being involved in the movement of money. And then the wider roles of the payment rail operator are optional in countries, it can be done by the regulator, or it can be done by a secondary team within the regulator. When it comes to data sharing, there's a few licensed roles that should be made really clear. Financial information providers and users, what are their obligations, roles and responsibilities to make sure that data sharing happens in a regulated manner. And second, an optional ecosystem operator, which is a self or a self regulatory organization that can work to sort of advocate for the technology standards, as well as hold and update the technology standards as needed. In some countries, this role is performed by the regulator. In other countries, you have separate entities that can do this. But thinking through clearly what are the roles in this instance allows you that flexibility. Finally, this role of a consent manager is one that exists in the India ecosystem, and not in the Brazilian ecosystem as a separate role. But you can think through whether or not you'd like to introduce a role of an entity that takes consent for data flows, without actually being involved in the data flow itself. On the technology specification, these are really critical to introduce. Without the introduction of these specifications, it's like bringing a knife to a gunfight. You come in to try to control what banks and technology players are doing. If you simply write regulations without backing them up with technology specs, it becomes very difficult to enforce those regulations in practice. This means that in code, by building a payments protocol, that's modern QR specification that you put out on data sharing, having separate APIs for data flows, and for data formats, which can then change by sector, as well as a consent specification, you can enforce the good practices that you put out in your regulation through code. And now, please can I hand the virtual mic over to Dr. Rao, Deputy Governor of the Reserve Bank of India. You have the floor. Thank you, Bill. I hope I'm audible to all of you. As a regulator, we look to frame and implement policies to minimize risk to the regulated entities and also ensure financial stability. At the same time, we also need to be open to innovations which can expand the remit of financial services in terms of scale, reach, and inclusion. And for this, I think technology plays a pivotal role as an enabler, which will make financial services more personalized, inclusive, accessible, and efficient. And open finance is the possible way ahead for this. Open finance is an extension of the concept of open banking. It empowers the customers who are the real data owners by giving them control over the financial data which is found in silos, so far available with the individual data holders and allow them to share it securely with authorized entities using third party consent based architecture so as to receive personalized and efficient financial services. So open finance has the potential to transform the financial landscape through finance as the lifestyle model, seamlessly integrating financial services into people's daily lives exactly when they need them with products and services that best meet their needs and receive the personal financial services they deserve. The thrust towards open financial finance initiatives in India started with the development of the digital public infrastructure. Today, it seeks to leverage on the interface of the three pillars of this, which is the identity, the payment, and the lending infrastructures, which have been or are in the process of being rolled out. The identification of the customer The identification of the customer begins with what we call the Jam Trinity, that is the Jandan account or the basic savings bank deposit account, which provides access to the formal financial services to the previously unbanked population. The Aadhaar card provides a digital identity to the Indian residents based on their biometric and demographic data, and a basic digital connectivity is ensured through affordable smartphones and internet connectivity. Together they have helped us in ensuring a revolution for underserved by enabling them to be part of the formal financial services. The payment infrastructure or the United Payments interface developed by our National Payments Council of India enables seamless digital payments that allow instant money transfer into multiple accounts using a mobile application or a QR code. This is now going to be complemented with the recently launched platform, which we have called as the Uniform Lending Interface, which is currently under trial on a pilot basis. This will enable frictionless disbursement of credit, bringing in efficiency into the lending ecosystem by reducing costs, ensuring speedy delivery and being scalable. This bouquet of DPI, that is the Jam, the UPI, and the ULI can be conceived of as the new trinity, which will revolutionize the formal financial landscape in India. The launch first took place of the UPI, which was followed by the account aggregator framework, which enables secure data sharing across financial institutions. The account aggregator framework has transformed data sharing by putting customers in control of their financial information with explicit consent as a requirement. And by maintaining control over their data privacy, customers can now decide who can access their data for what purpose and for how long. This approach empowers the individuals to share their financial information across, which is spread across various financial institutions with other financial entities to receive personalized services. Unlike traditional models for financial institutions held data in isolated silos, the account aggregator framework ensures that the data is securely shared and that too only for consented purpose. Another crucial regulatory feature of this framework is that while managing the consent of the customer and transferring data to the financial information user, the account aggregators do not store or access the data themselves. This design is intended at ensuring the misuse is minimized or sensitive financial information is not abused. And so there is a greater degree of data security and trust for the customer. This is also in line with the legal framework where we have the Digital Personal Data Protection Act, which has been passed by the parliament, which recognizes the right of individuals to protect their data, and also the need to process the data for lawful purposes. I think two key features of this framework, which I thought I would highlight. One is its cross regulatory collaboration is involved in this process. The account aggregator framework not only has comprehensive coverage, spanning about 19 different types of financial information, but it also integrates with entities regulated by the various sectoral regulators, that is the Reserve Bank, the SEBI, the Insurance Regulation Development Authority and the Pension Fund Regulation Development Authority as well as the government departments. Second is a principle based approach to address the inclusion enables us to have this as an adaptive and flexible framework. So if you want to add in new systems into the new forms of asset types, they can be easily integrated into the account aggregator system. Recently, we included the goods and services tax network of India as a financial information provider, which now facilitates cash flow based lending to micro, small and medium enterprises. Having said that, I think there are a few challenges to which perhaps are need to be addressed to unlock the potential of this ecosystem. Now, one, we need an interoperable and inclusive account aggregator system. Some entities are reluctant to integrate with all account aggregators, given perhaps constraints in terms of bandwidth or the resources they have to spare. So that can actually impact the system's overall effectiveness and reach. So this reluctance poses a challenge and perhaps a fully interoperable and inclusive account aggregators ecosystem will perhaps be needed to ensure that this constraint is overcome. Then second is a lack of public awareness to the degree which is desirable. So despite the framework's potential, the public awareness is still a bit low. Increasing the understanding about the concept of consent and trust among the consumers is critical for widespread adoption. To address these issues, actually, we in the Reserve Bank have taken the responsibility of building awareness about the account aggregator framework through public awareness campaigns. Having said that, I think some key issues which need to be addressed. One is honoring the trust of the customer. Trust, once compromised, is exceptionally challenging to restore. And that is an example which all of us in the financial sector know very well. There have been rising concerns recently about frequency with which certain entities access data. So that raises the question of whether the data is being used strictly for the consented purpose or for some other purpose. It is essential that the financial information is accessed and used solely for the purposes which are explicitly authorized by the customer. This is not just a matter of compliance, it's about honoring the trust of the customer. Adhering to fair usage principles is crucial for protecting customer privacy and maintaining trust, which are fundamental to the long-term success and effectiveness of this account aggregator framework. Let me just conclude by saying that this inclusive open finance infrastructure has tremendous second-order impacts on the economy in terms of formalization, in terms of financial stability, effectiveness of monetary policy, Kamiya just mentioned, and also controlled innovation. While challenges remain, the increased participation in the account aggregator system coupled with Reserve Bank's commitment to facilitate the new trinity, I think, lays the foundation for a good future for the open finance in India. However, the success of this ambitious vision depends on several factors. First and foremost, it requires responsible engagement of the regulated entities, which will ensure that the framework operates with the integrity and transparency so that not only it meets but also exceeds its potential, which will drive a more inclusive, competitive, and innovative financial future in India. So, of course, the BIS, we work with our member central banks and other stakeholders, and including the RBI and the Central Bank of Brazil, which both governors have shared their experiences. We also try to explore some of these new dimensions through our innovation activities. One of the projects we are soon will launch, which is dealing with our cross-border open finance infrastructures. This is a joint project with many central banks. To my knowledge, RBI and Central Bank of Brazil also joined the project. So, I'm not, you know, this is, since this is the closing remarks, I'm not going to get into the details to the project. Instead, let me have a little bit of reflections on what I learned or what is my main takeaway out of today's discussions. So, firstly, in my view, there have been significant barriers to financial inclusions in most economies, but particularly in emerging markets. They emanate from societal attitude, legal framework, and higher transaction costs. You know, as the technology progressed, particularly in the last decade and a half, carefully designed DPIs, including open finance architectures, have emerged, which provide a potential way forward for countries to leapfrog traditional stages of development in this area. That's one takeaway. Secondary, in terms of the financial development, as my BIS colleague mentioned today, a collateral gap, because the large percentage of the world's populations, including the SME, small businesses, even if they do have bank accounts, but normally they cannot borrow funds because of the inadequate, tangible collateral. So that's a collateral gap. So data and activities collected by digital platforms in this regard, you know, can help to overcome these difficulties by bridging the gap and creating information as a new capital. Thirdly, we have heard how open finance can support financial stability. So the stability is one of the important dimensions for central banks. A open and efficient financial system can foster robust competition, encourage new entrants, and keep existing service providers nimble. So these are very good development, or encouraging development, that will promote continuous innovations within these financial industries and hopefully lower the cost for customers. Finally, I want to mention that while open finance offers quite a wide range of benefits, it also potentially poses significant risks and concerns. I think both governors mentioned it, particularly around the privacies and data security. And of course, as we all discussed, robust governance will remain essential. So that's pretty much the major takeaway I wanted to share at this moment. But before closing, let me take this opportunity to once again thank the CCAF and the CDBI for co-hosting this conference with us. And in particular, I would like to thank Nick Clark for giving such enlightening opening remarks, and Bill Robert for his excellent moderations of the panel discussions at the end, as well as, of course, Kamya Chandler for her thoughts on the global best practice in open finance technology architectures. And of course, my appreciation goes to all the speakers, Deputy Governors Rao and Deputy Governor Damaso for your insightful views and sharing of the lessons learned from your own countries. And of course, last, my thanks go to all the participants for tonight's conference. With that, let me close the conference. I wish you a pleasant weekend. And thank you. See you next time.

In 2020, Brazil's central bank introduced PIX, an instant payment system that revolutionized digital transactions. In just two years, PIX doubled the number of people using digital payments. This is their story. Since 2014, we have been signaling to the market the need for an instant payment system that could make transactions at low cost through smartphones, cell phones. But the market began to emerge with closed solutions and no open solutions. 77% of purchases were made in cash, which has a very high cost for the country. So the main goal was to stimulate the electronization of payments. We detected some problems in the payment industry in Brazil. It is very important to stress that this model brings a combination of our physical identity document with our digital document, with our digital identity, which is the GovBR account. This is the biggest difficulty in digital transformation in most countries, the integration of databases. You can pull the information from all databases to leverage their use, both from a social and economic development point of view. The principles that we will try to internalize in this process are free software, interoperability, modularity, connectivity, simplicity and transparency. Without this, you can't do it.
