   23. Bringing Data Sharing at Scale to Life – 1 | Amit Ranjan (Ex-Architect of Digilocker) | Masterclass  
        [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4GAn0927tMQ](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4GAn0927tMQ) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4GAn0927tMQ&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> I was one of the founders for SlideShare and SlideShare was acquired by LinkedIn in 2012 and I continued working there for a couple of years and in 2014 was when I was exiting SlideShare and LinkedIn and it so happened that I had always been connected with the product ecosystem in India, having volunteered for many industry bodies like NASSCOM, like iSpirit, and it was through their connection that they wanted some help with specifically iSpirit. I was a volunteer with iSpirit right from their inception and they wanted some help with a government consultation, so because I had some free time with me, so I was helping them out. I went and visited the Ministry of Electronics and IT and that was my first brush with the government actually and that was also when I believe the government got to know about me and my background for the first time. So Aadhaar happened in 2008 or 2009 and by 2014 or 15, there were already close to 60 to 70 crore Aadhaar cards or Aadhaar numbers that have been issued. So Aadhaar was in the bag in that sense and the government of the day was kind of considering that now that we have Aadhaar and one of the key things about Aadhaar is that Aadhaar is built as an API and the whole concept and the purpose of making it an API is that you can build things on top of it, things that leverage and use Aadhaar's API capabilities. So the government was in a broad sense thinking what are the other products and services that a citizen could actually utilize in their daily lives and they were thinking about something in the payment space, they were thinking something about in the data space. So that was the juncture when I joined. I think they had some very brief and sketchy ideas about something in the document and the data space, but specifically DigiLocker hadn't taken shape at that point in time. What DigiLocker does essentially, if you think of it, it is a key citizen pain point. We are all used to this, we all have various documents, certificates, mark sheets and IDs that are issued by the government or some agency of the government over our lifetime and we tend to use them in a repetitive manner. We take photographies and then we submit it. So we are all familiar with that ecosystem and the problems associated with it. Now given the fact that the internet had happened by then, Adhaar's capabilities were well known by that time. So I think you can add one and one together and say, okay, what is it that we can now build using the existing technological capabilities to solve a particular citizen pain point. And in this case, the pain point being this repetitive use of papers and certificates and IDs, which is so slow, bureaucratic, it causes wastage. You can imagine it leads to delays. So if there was a digital way of solving that problem, that is what the government was thinking in terms of the DigiLocker plan. DigiLocker, I think one of the easiest ways to think of it, I think the idea is borrowed or similar to what a Dropbox or a Google Drive is, with one difference that in a Dropbox or a Google Drive, you essentially upload your own documents. So you are the master of what you upload and any authenticity of those documents comes essentially from what you intend it to be. DigiLocker is kind of slightly different and that's where the real power comes in. The documents are essentially coming from their respective government sources. So like for example, our school certificates and our mark sheets are issued by the state education boards. The PAN database, the PAN number that we have is issued by CBDT. Our passport is issued by the Ministry of External Affairs. So what happens is that with each of these data records or IDs, they have been issued by some wing or some department or something which comes from the government side. So what DigiLocker does is that it's built a central thin application, it's a thin layer which is talking in turn to each of these individual data sources and the citizen can actually fetch their citizen records via the DigiLocker platform. But DigiLocker does not copy that data. So that is a very key architectural design decision is that DigiLocker does not have the data itself, DigiLocker does not copy the data. DigiLocker technically, it's what you would call a consent manager and consent managers are only about managing the data exchange. They cannot look at the data, they cannot manipulate the data, they cannot change the data. So the whole idea is that these documents or these data records that are being aggregated on the platform, they have to be used in a certain workflow or a certain use case as part of maybe a government process. So on one hand, you have the issuers of these records, like as I said, the education boards or let's say the CVDT or the MEA or could be the driving license, which is issued by Ministry of Transport. So they are the data issuers in the system and then there are the data users or the data consumers. So for example, if I as a citizen, I'm driving on the road and the cop stops me and says, hey, can you show me your driving license? So the driving license is being asked for by the cop on location. So they are a data consumer and what DigiLocker enables is that I can, instead of showing my physical driving license, I can actually take out my mobile app, whip out the DigiLocker app, and from there I can show the digital record for the driving license to the cop. And there's a whole legal system and a legal structure that has been built around it because you can build an application, but how do you ensure that it is recognized as a legally verifiable credential? It is something that the courts will agree to. It is something that the cop on the street, when he or she is shown this particular document, how do you ensure that they are going to believe that this is the authentic thing? So that is the whole power of DigiLocker is that if it's coming from DigiLocker, it has to be secure, it has to be original, it has to be authentic because DigiLocker is directly talking to the source databases and it is enabling the data exchange for the data consumers. It's verifiable because that's the whole point of DigiLocker is that if it's on DigiLocker, it is verified as coming from the original source of truth, which means it is credible, it is validated and you don't need to revalidate that, that is this the right record. If it is on DigiLocker, it is verified. Coming from the background of startups working in the government is that a lot of what we build and it's very easy to think of the primary users coming from a background where they can just do it themselves. It's a DIY use scale or it's a self use case, but if you go to the villages, if you go to the semi-urban areas, if you go to absolutely remote upcountry areas, a lot of people there don't have the access or the means to access that or even if they let's say had a mobile phone, they may not know how to navigate through the app stores, download it, verify it. So the government often what they do in these situations is that there is an assisted mode of delivery. For example, in the rural areas, the government has something called the CSC centers and the CSC centers, somebody who's living in the villages can actually go to the CSC center and help and they will be helped in setting up their DigiLocker account and if they want to kind of take out a printout or show that document to somebody, they can do it via the CSC center. So there are different modes of operation here. There is the self-serve mode, there is the DIY mode and there is the assisted mode. QR codes have become ubiquitous now, especially after UPI, where it's become very common for people and people understand now that QR codes are a way of verifying the authenticity. Similarly, the same concept has been used in DigiLocker, wherein the certificates have a QR code and if you wanted to verify that, okay, is this certificate genuine, then the QR code can be read by the QR reader in the app itself and that kind of validates that this is the original. Multiple ways in which we approach the design of the system. So I think the first and foremost is that building something from the government at population scale. So concerns like privacy, security and consent is at the heart of the system. I would use the word consent is something which is key here because DigiLocker also, as I said, essentially is a consent manager. And the government of India has a very important framework called the consent framework. So DigiLocker follows the consent framework. And in a broader sense, there is something called the DEPA, which is the Data Empowerment and Protection Architecture. So DigiLocker is kind of part and parcel of the DEPA framework. And essentially what the consent framework essentially says is that the data belongs to the citizens and the data is available in DigiLocker only and only if the citizen kind of wants it and the citizen wants to share it with a respective data consumer. So it is not possible to access anybody's data unless and until the citizen is in the center of that flow and the loop and they authorize and they consent to it and they say that, okay, I allow my data coming from this source to be used by this source. And you can specify for so much time and so much, so the multiple things they can be specified like. So I would say that is probably one of the first things that consent is at the heart of the system. I think in terms of DPI, most systems are built on open standards. There is extensive use of open source, open APIs are a big part of the DPI architecture and the framework. And you must understand that governments by definition are built in silos and that is by design. So data by design is fragmented, it is siloed, the left arm and the right arm do not talk to each other. So in many ways, one of the real challenges that we faced building something like DigiLocker is that in a situation where the design itself is of data fragmentation, how do you build some kind of an aggregation layer? And that is only by ensuring that the product and the platform, it kind of completely takes care of data, security, privacy, consent to the highest order and the highest level. Building something of this magnitude, of this scale cannot be done without overcoming the challenges. I think the first thing that comes to my mind is that, so this is kind of, and especially when going back to 2014-15, this was a futuristic idea, futuristic concept. It was an unproven concept. And so one of the things that you see happening is that, so there is this dichotomy between where are the legal provisions, where is the law, and what is the technology kind of trying to achieve? And is it allowed by law? And what has happened is that technology is kind of galloping at its own pace. It is keeping far ahead of law. So that I think kind of turns out to be a real challenge, is that you are building something at a very fast pace. And at a time, technology does tend to be slightly ahead of the law. But you know, once you kind of have something, you need to dial back and make sure that it conforms to whatever the legal provisions are. So I think that is something that comes up frequently when you are building these kind of API, DPI platforms, is that not everything may be kind of streamlined as far as the legal provisions are concerned on the day you start building it. There is a process of iterative understanding and incremental development that is happening. And soon they kind of catch up and they are in sync with each other. So I would say that is one of the key things that challenges that we faced. Right, I think, you know, across the world, digitization is something which has picked up space, both on the private side, as well as the government or the public side. And what my personal experience with DigiLocker has been that the use case for DigiLocker is pretty like, pretty, it's at the least common denominator level. You can pick up any country and they would have some kind of system whereby there is a digital, you know, version of the school certificates and mark sheets or driving licenses or their tax IDs. So every country probably has the need for a basic fundamental level data and document platform. So that is something that we have seen all across. What the beauty of India's stack is that it kind of gives you a complete architecture and a framework for any country that really wants to kind of, you know, build a digital stack over its citizen services, then how do you kind of go about doing it? You start off with some kind of a digital ID, then you can actually link that digital ID to something like a data platform, then you can maybe, you know, have a payment related system that is kind of built on top of it. So, and the whole value of the stack kind of, you know, increases exponentially once you have these various platforms working in conjunction with one another. I mean, you can look at the platforms in isolation, but the real power of the stack is when you actually kind of put them together and then you can see that the value actually expands exponentially.








   24. Bringing Data Sharing at Scale to Life-2 | Abhishek Singh (Additional Secretary, MeitY) | MasterClass  
        [https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8nH\_2BmZgXE](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8nH_2BmZgXE) [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8nH_2BmZgXE&utm_source=chatgpt.com)


**Video Transcript:**
> The entire DPI story is like a story of digital transformation for India. And DigiLocker as a DPI was part of the bouquet of the services that Ministry of Electronics and IT was working along with the other stakeholders in order to ensure more efficient public services to citizens, in order to ensure, reduce the hardship that people face, in order to reduce the dependence on paper for most documents and certificates that people need, and in order to ensure that documents can be easily accessed and verified by whosoever we need it. I, of course, happened to get into this role because of my assignment. We are civil servants. The government posted me as the head of National E-Governance Division, Digital India Corporation, which was at the forefront of implementing this project. And this was, of course, probably based on my earlier experience also having worked in this ministry in 2009-12 as a Deputy Secretary and Director for E-Governance. See, like one of the most difficult things that people find, especially when they are accessing public services, is number one, to prove who they are, to prove where they stay. So, if suppose you are wanting admission for a child in a school, in nursery, the school will immediately want you for a birth certificate. Birth certificate is issued by a public authority. So, you go there, you either queue up in that office or you fill up an online form, get the birth certificate, either a paper certificate or a PDF certificate. Then for the school admission portal, you go to the Education Ministry or the private school, public school, whichever website. Then you apply, even if you are applying online, you have to upload a PDF also. So, effectively what is happening is that a citizen, both the two services, the issuance of a certificate and the need for accessing a certificate, both are required by a government entity or an authority. But in effect, rather than the two systems talking to each other, we are reducing the citizen to act as a courier between the two systems to apply, get a certificate from one authority and go and submit to another authority. And this whole reliance of documents, and then very often most public authorities would want to submit documents in triplicate, sometimes they will want it to be attested by a Gazette Officer. So, ultimately the citizen spends a lot of time and energy in moving from one office to the other office, collecting all kinds of documents, getting it attested, verified, submitted. And even if a document is attested, the department or the office which is accepting it does not even know that it is attested by a genuine officer or something. So, a lot of forgery, a lot of fake documents will start coming in. So, it was felt that if we have to enable ease of living for citizens and ease of doing business for businesses, we will have to solve this problem. So, how do we ensure that documents are issued electronically, documents are issued are accessed electronically and any third party who wants to verify a document can they verify electronically. So, this was the premise on which the DigiLocker was built. And the architecture is very simple, the issuer gives their APIs to DigiLocker, so that DigiLocker can access the documents. And any verifier, when they want to verify a document, whether what he is submitting is correct or not, then he makes a query, consent is given by the owner of the document and then the access is provided for API based verification of documents. So, whether it is academic documents, whether driving licenses, whether it is Aadhaar document, whether income tax papers or health records, all have become part of this entire ecosystem, which enables paperless governance and which enables electronic authentication verification of documents by all stakeholders. It results in benefit to the citizens, it results in benefit for the issuers, they do not need to print paper documents and most importantly it results in benefit for the end users who want to verify, who want to authenticate any document that has been issued through this system. The most important component when it was designed and it was architected was number one, there was no intent or there is no feature for storing documents centrally. It was built on a federated architecture approach, wherein documents will ultimately reside on the issuer of the document. So for example, if CBSE is issuing class 10th and class 12th certificates, it is accessible on DigiLocker, but this does not mean that all the certificates are coming to a central database or central data center or central repository, the certificates do not come there. We only get access through APIs of the documents and we pull it and make it available to the user of the document. Similarly for driving licenses, similarly for Aadhaar, similarly for PAN card, similarly for all records which are part of that. And similarly when verification happens, the consent framework for DigiLocker was notified by MIT way back in 2015 and based on that, any verifier, anyone who wants to verify the document can kind of access it through APIs and do the verification. So actual document is not flowing into a central repository or going to a verifier. Only the verification is happening electronically, which ensures in a very secure, very simple design. And the second part what has happened is that, like over and above, like as we move forward, very often people wanted like kind of an offline verification also. So what we did was that we introduced a QR code in each document. So QR code can be scanned by the QR code scanner on the DigiLocker app and the authenticity of the document is proved. So for example, when you are going to the airport and you do not have your paper ID document and the CISF person at the airport wants you to show your ID, then you can show your DigiLocker ID on the DigiLocker app. But that also, he can also scan the QR code and verify it from the server. So that way the design was made to ensure that it results in ease of use by the users, ease of verification and it works that way. And the third thing that DigiLocker also did was that it exposed the APIs, the verification APIs to various users. So for the entire ecosystem of users, what we call as a DigiLocker partners, there are FinTech companies, there are mutual fund companies who are kind of doing their KYC by using the DigiLocker credentials. So when they have to onboard users and they want to verify the identity of a user, they do it through the DigiLocker APIs. So that results in their cost, the cost of KYC coming down, the ease of onboarding users is there and the numbers go up. For example, Zaroda CEO has been on record, Nitin Kamath, saying that how earlier it used to take a lot of time to acquire a customer, a cost and the time taken. And very often if you have to open a DMAT account, you would require to sign maybe 40 places you have to sign on paper documents. But with DigiLocker, the KYC thing became very easy. You could do a Aadhaar based KYC through the DigiLocker and then onboarding of users became easy. The cost of acquiring a customer came down drastically. The time taken to onboard a user came down drastically and resulted in a lot of benefits. In fact, most of the FinTech unicorns today, of course, they are providing a lot of services also. But part of the reason for they becoming unicorns and they acquiring more users and they providing the services also is because of the ease of adding customers that happens with the DigiLocker APIs. Similarly, there have been other uses. For example, everyone would have used DigiYatra. That also works on a very simple DigiLocker based API verification that happens. So the simple design and architecture ensured that it was easy for people to use and it was also easy for application developers to integrate DigiLocker APIs in their system and design. And that is why even though it may appear that it is not very popular as like UPI, but UPI today has maybe around 300 million users and DigiLocker has 280 million users. So we are not too far behind. Yes, that is what that is the beauty of the verifiable credentials. What happens is that most people are not very literate. They will not be able to decipher and you need documents in various languages and all. But QR code is something which cannot be fudged. QR code is something wherein it pulls the information directly from the server. So when we did the COVID vaccination certificates, there also the verifiable credentials approach was used and it was done on a standard which is becoming a global standard for the verifiable credentials. So if I have my certificates, if I have the QR code on my certificate, it could be verified by any entity whether in India or anywhere in the world. So that is what is now going to come in the academic documents also. So it does not matter whether you are an urban citizen or a rural citizen, a student from a village or a student from a public school in the city, all of you will get the same academic credentials, the same QR code, QR code with a similar QR code, which can be scanned by the DigiLocker scanner and verified. And now with the academic bank of credits and APAR ID coming in, wherein all academic credentials, even your semester mark sheets of colleges, universities becoming a part of the National Academic Depository on the DigiLocker. These documents can be verified by universities and by employers to give you jobs. So that all the time that was taken earlier for verifying your credentials, you are verifying your transcripts, that is going to reduce a lot. And what I feel is that going forward, this will become the universal global standard and other countries will also like to build something like this. Already there has been talk of Apple decided to introduce Apple Wallet in California and became big news and they were wanting to introduce driving licenses on that when we had been doing this for the last six years or so. So other countries are also looking at it. There are a lot of advantages to that. It is climate friendly because you reduce paper and it also results in a lot of benefits to users, to stakeholders, to authenticators, to everyone. Multiple challenges because to convince partners to join the DigiLocker ecosystem has been a huge, huge challenge. Per se, the technology is very simple. APIs are used in almost every application today. So the simple technology is very simple, but the effort required to onboard users and the effort required to get ministries and departments issue orders and notifications saying that DigiLocker documents are as authentic as paper-based documents. That was a major effort. So like we started with that for identity, it became like banking. RBI issued guidelines which said that KYC documents coming from DigiLocker can be used for opening bank accounts. The CB also came up with that, that could be used for opening DMAT accounts. Railways came with an order which says that these documents can be used for verifying people to travel in trains. The Ministry of Road Transport and Highways said that a driving license on a DigiLocker is as authentic as a paper driving license. And then a host of institutions have issued such orders which has made DigiLocker get legal sanctity. And then the effort that the team does, our team which works on that, one part is the technology team and one part is the partnerships team. So the partnerships team works with various boards. For example, school boards. There are 28 to 29 school boards across the country. To convince each one of them to bring their certificates on this was a huge, huge effort. Then once they saw the value in that, they became onboarded. So for the universities, for example, when we are building the academic bank of credits and the APAR IDs, we are working with all the thousand plus universities across the country to get them to get onboarded on them, to get them to give their APIs here, to get them to agree to issue their certificates, mark sheets through this. It had been a huge effort. In fact, some universities thought that they will, because very often when they give the copies of degrees or when they give transcripts, they charge money for that. So they started feeling that they will lose a revenue stream. Then we had to convince that the benefits will far outweigh what you will lose. And Department of Higher Education also chipped in and said that like once you get onboarded, whatever revenue loss you will have in giving paper certificates will compensate you. In National Health Authority, when they are building the Ayushman Bharat Digital Mission, when the health records, the electronic health records came in, they have said that there can be several health lockers where electronic health records can be stored. But DigiLocker was the first port of call. And today, I think DigiLocker is the only robust health locker that is existing in the ABDM ecosystem, where in electronic health records, your blood reports, your X-ray reports, your MRI reports, your CT scan reports, all can be stored on the DigiLocker in an electronic format. And if you want to take a second opinion from some doctor and want to share those documents, you can digitally share with documents of any hospital with any hospital. So that's again something that's very, very kind of transformational. And bringing all these partners and onboarding them and getting government orders issued with regard to the legal sanctity of the whole document, the DigiLocker ecosystem has been, I would say, maybe the more important part, rather than just building the technology part of it. Globally, more and more countries are planning to adopt. And one use case that I feel and I hope will be done is that the entire passport visa system can get integrated on a DigiLocker kind of system. Because like every country, when you are giving a visa to a visitor from some other country, the one thing that you want to verify is that he has a genuine passport and you have know the identity of that. So if the entire passport visa ecosystem globally starts following common standards and all countries start using that, then if it comes on a DigiLocker ecosystem, then it has great value and if it's integrated with the VFS and the BLI systems which issue the passports and with the visas, then you maybe when you are applying for a visa, you will not need to physically give your passports in an embassy. So that kind of thing will be very, very transformational. Similarly, academic certificates. Academic certificates are something which everybody needs. There is a lot of mobility in the education space. People go from India to study in the United States. People go from all parts of countries to study all over the world. So everywhere you need to verify the transcripts. So if this becomes like a global standard, verification of academic credentials across the world will become very, very easy. Similarly, for employment, people have the work experience, educational qualifications, that becomes the basis for applying for a job. So if your academic credentials or if your work experience is verified digitally, then getting these jobs will become very, very easy. Then the entire space of business, for example, smart contracts, for example, agreements, documents, various kinds of MOUs, agreements that people share, if all of it becomes part of this ecosystem, verifying them, authenticating them becomes very, very easy. And further, the work that we are doing along with CBSE and the educational ecosystem along with the DigiLocker team is bringing all these certificates on a blockchain-based architecture so that the authenticity of the document with regard to it being tamper-proof, it being authentic is also further ensured. So all this I feel that in the next three to five years, this will evolve greatly. The system will become much more secure than what it is now. System will become much more replicable. There are already several countries who have shown interest in replicating a DigiLocker kind of system in their own countries, including countries like the United Kingdom. And I will feel that once this becomes more ubiquitous, once countries start using it for their own domestic needs to begin with, the next step will be to build up a document cloud across the world and all documents which are required for international travel, that will also become part of this ecosystem and will result in a lot of benefit for the economy of the world for businesses to do their transactions seamlessly without the need of any paper. That's why DigiLocker follows the best of the security standards. I believe it's ISO 27001 to which it confirms. And even though we are not storing data of citizens as issued from the publishers at a central repository, still the API-based transactions are happening. So data does travel when the API-based access is done. So that is also done in an encrypted format, that's done in a fully secure format, so that if there is an attack or if there is somebody who is trying to meddle into the APIs and trying to steal data from that, that is also prevented. So that kind of system, that kind of architecture, that kind of security design has been built in, which ensures that all the data that remains on the DigiLocker ecosystem is 100% secure without any issue. Regular audits are done, regular security checks are done in order to ensure there are no patches which are left behind. Sometimes our own teams are subject to try to do a hackathon or try to do a cyberstorm in order to say that what if some attack happens, what if something goes wrong, how do we ensure that we are secure. So all those things are part of our regular business practice, which to keep the DigiLocker data and information fully secure. There is another aspect to DigiLocker that apart from the data, that information that is issued by issuers, the authentic document, DigiLocker also provides 1 GB of space to all users, wherein one can store my personal documents also. So there also a lot of security is ensured that if I am keeping my personal documents under the DigiLocker, those cannot of course be verified because they are my own personal documents that I am uploading. But still it kind of allows you a document wallet so that I can carry my personal document like a drive anywhere. But there also 100% security is ensured that that data that I also upload is kept very secure and is like any attack that happens can be registered or can be checked and can be ensured that 100% security and privacy of data is ensured. See something similar would not be needed. What we need to do is that a startups and all we have almost 2000 partners who are there who are building applications on top of DigiLocker because what DigiLocker has brought is like all important documents about the citizens life cycle available on one space. So there is a lot of value in building applications in the financial space, in the health space on top of DigiLocker. And last year in fact the EntityLocker also introduced wherein not only users but companies, organizations, trusts, not for profits also can open a DigiLocker account wherein they can keep their documents relating to their tax returns relating to their company filings on a DigiLocker ecosystem. The businesses also have a lot to gain. So I would say that any startup or any business who is trying to build a public service they can easily access the DigiLocker APIs, use it for the KYC, use it for verifying information on that which is available on the DigiLocker ecosystem, build applications on top of it, offer value added services and they will create a lot of value with this. There are a lot of countries who are looking at it, Sri Lanka is looking at it, Mauritius is looking at it, United Kingdom is looking at it, the United States have had a look at it. So we are sharing them the experience that we have had both from the technology point of view and both on the effort that has been required to build the ecosystem around DigiLocker. So that is what we engage as part of the DPI diplomacy that is going on. The global DPI repository, DigiLocker is an important component of the global DPI repository and we have signed MOUs also with nine countries. So it is a work in progress wherein the insights with regard to building a system like DigiLocker is being shared with the international community for those who want to learn, replicate and build something similar to DigiLocker.

So when we did that exercise, we found that there are more than 2000 companies in India which are making software. So that means there is a department that's been identified in the government. There's a budget behind it. I think you would have talked about, for example, for finance, how the NPCI does that for UPI, how UIDI does that for the identity system in India or other. The National Health Authority is the one that does the digital public infrastructure for health in India. And what does it do? It essentially has three big digital public goods that we have identified. The very first one is called a Health Information Exchange and Consent Manager. It's called a HICM. And its primary role is three things. The first is it's expected to help everybody get access and build their own personal health records, irrespective of which health care provider they go to. So that's number one. Number two, it's supposed to provide a certain set of data. Completely, these are all aggregate data sets. So there is no personal information in it, which helps them do disease surveillance and interesting information for public health management. So classic example is COVID. You really couldn't manage COVID if you couldn't tell where there is a positive test case happening, how many cases were coming in different areas, and so on. So this is the kind of public health data that's quite important. And the last bit is where we are trying to create anonymized data sets that can be very useful for AI. So that's the first digital public good. The second digital public infrastructure is to do with health insurance. So this one just looks at how do you move information, digital information, between a provider and a payer, if there are scenarios where there are payers. So in India, interestingly, unlike many, I don't know how it is in various countries, but most countries will usually cover both inpatient care and outpatient care through insurance, if they have an insurance scheme. India, almost all insurance schemes are only for inpatient care. Outpatient care is typically out of pocket. So people pay cash when they're doing outpatient care. And they'll usually have a claim when they have an inpatient episode in India. But this digital public good is for optimizing and standardizing that interchange so that providers can get money and can actually track how much money they have to get. And the last digital public good is for digital health services. So think about if you want to book an appointment with a doctor, is it going to be a standard experience across all health care providers, or it's going to vary? How do you do teleconsultation? So for example, in India, and in most cases, teleconsultation required both the doctor and the patient to use the same software provider. So that's how they were achieving it. In India, we have actually tried to make sure that doctors can use any software they want, patients can use any software they want. If they are UHI, as we call it, UHI compliant, it's interoperable, you can actually do a full telemedicine consultation, which actually means you can find a doctor, you can book an appointment when they're free, you can make a payment that the doctor wants, you can exchange health records, you can do the video consultation, and you can get your prescription back. All of this completely interoperably using this interaction. So those are the three big digital public goods that the government in India is trying to bring to the market. Now I'm not going to get into the details of all of them. I'm going to just step through a few so that you guys can get an idea of how these things work and then take it from there. Now not everything, in fact I need to update these slides, these two just went live. So we have a staged process to actually make this happen. So we start by building the digital public goods, inviting ecosystem players to come and integrate, start rolling it out, and that's when we usually say it's gone live. So this is live and these two just went live. They came out of the sandbox and went live. We have a couple which are still in design phases, so we have not yet actually rolled it out into production in India. So this is also a digital public journey that's evolving. Of course our identity journey and our payment journey are much older. So the payment journey, for example, started in 2015. This exercise started in 2018 and was actually started to implement. The government said let's do it in the middle of the COVID pandemic, in actually 2020. So it's only about three to four years old in terms of its journey. Now in general, and I don't know how it is in your countries, but I have not seen a lab report in India that wasn't like a printed report, which means it really came off a computer from somewhere, right? So health information is quickly going digital. So if I look at the Indian context and I think if I look at many of the other countries' context, you will still probably find doctors who like to talk to the patient and write a prescription on a paper, maybe tear it away and give it up. But when it comes to other things like lab reports or many other things, digitization is actually happening quite fast, and that's also very, very valuable information. So in India it's quite common that if you go to a hospital, they will do a lab test and after that you will get a little message on your phone, and then you can actually click on the message and you can view your report as a PDF on your mobile. I don't know how, can I have a show of hands how many countries who are here have it in their countries? Do you guys get access to, yeah, one. Nobody else? Nobody's even getting, yeah. So quite a few. So now the problem, what's the problem with this? The big issue or the problem with this is, approach is, that we are not actually taking this digital information and making it to a longitudinal patient history. So if I want you to find that report again after a couple of months, it's unlikely that you will find it. And in the context of with a doctor, it doesn't happen. The second problem is, the PDF is not something that's, it's easy for us as humans to read, it's easy for us to interpret and say, okay, this is what the report is. But let's say if I gave you the reports from multiple labs for the same parameter and say, okay, let's look at a trend of this. It's not that easy, unless you have some amount of machine readability into the document itself. So that's really what we are trying to do with this mission. So one of the key things, and this I'm talking about from the first digital public good on the left, the health information exchange and consent manager. So what we do is, we tell healthcare providers, hey, Mr. Healthcare provider, if you have a piece of health information that belongs to a patient and you want to share it digitally with the patient, do that by linking it to their health ID. So interesting word here, in India, that health ID is called an Aishman Bharat Health Account. So we use the word account instead of an ID because we want to kind of give the idea that we are depositing health information into your account. And so that's the idea. And the keyword here is linking, and we'll come to that as to why we have taken that as an approach. So no health information actually gets deposited, only a link to a health information gets deposited in your health account. And second, we say, okay, the information you're sharing, can you make it into a standardized format? So we have chosen an international standard that's called FHIR, which stands for Fast Health Interoperability Resources, to standardize the format of the documents that are kind of getting linked. So that's really what we are trying to do here. And what do we allow them to do? We can actually share all of these kind of things. So you can share diagnostic reports, you can share discharge summaries, OP consultation notes, prescriptions, immunization records, all of this can be linked to an Aabha. And it's by design. Actually there were many, many more that we could have started with. We intentionally chose the most common ones, the most useful ones in healthcare, and so we started with minimal information. And we also chose things that healthcare patients, sorry, providers were happy to share with their patients. So to give you an example of what is not shared, in India, for example, if you stay in a hospital, the hospital creates, by law, much more information, including, for example, your OT notes. They create something for every day of your stay, especially when you're in ICU. All of this they keep with them and they call it indoor records. They don't like sharing it unless you ask for it. And so we have also picked the ones that they are comfortable sharing so that we can get the ecosystem started. Now I said that all of this gets linked into something we are calling as a health account or a health ID. So how do patients actually get that? What we have given is we have created a whole bunch of apps. So in UPI, in the payment ecosystem, you saw private providers who were creating apps. So similarly in the health ecosystem, we have invited private parties to come in and build consumer apps. We call these consumer apps as PHR apps or personal health record apps. And there's one app from the government. So this app here you see as Aarogya Setu. That's the app that the government is promoting. All the other apps that you see are apps that is coming from the private ecosystem. So we want to encourage competition, better design, better solutions for consumers from the private ecosystem. So you can download any one of these apps and in those apps you can actually create your health account. So it takes just a few seconds for you to actually create your health account. And we also have the whole system designed for people who carry absolutely no phones or have only feature phones. So we have a card that you can get at a hospital if you are somebody who does not carry a phone or have only a feature phone. And that card actually has a little QR code that you can show and use when you actually want to collect your health record. So that's the core idea. So it's inclusive in its thinking. Even if you don't have a phone, you will be able to collect your records digitally into your longitudinal records and you will be able to share it for your health care. Now if you go to any of the hospitals, you guys are in Bangalore. In fact, this is rolled out in many public hospitals in Bangalore. If you go to a public hospital in Bangalore, you will see, you can see a little QR code that's pasted at the entrance of the hospital. So at the registration desk, you have, just like you have for payments, I'm sure you've already done, tomorrow you're doing demos, but okay. So I'm sure in India you have seen how easy we do payments with scanning a QR code. We have borrowed that for health patient registration. So when you go into a hospital, you just scan your QR and you're instantly registered in the hospital software. So you are using a PHR app. So as a patient, you could be having any of these PHR apps. The hospital is using some software that you don't, of course, know, but you can just go scan and they will instantly register you. And we have more than a hundred thousand patients who use this to register themselves now. Every day we call this scan and share. So A, it saves, you know, no typing because, you know, the information is coming automatically. And actually we found that in public hospitals, which had very long queues, it was able to cut the queue time down. You know, people would wait about 60 minutes for a registration. Now with this scan, they're able to do it in about four minutes. So huge benefit. One of the reasons why people are using it is because they don't have to stand in the queue for registering themselves. Right? Now, what would I really want you to remember? The core idea that ABDM is doing is something very simple. If a healthcare provider is generating a medical record, what we said is we want them to link it with their ABHA. And then if a healthcare provider wants to view the records linked with the ABHA, it can only be done with the patient's consent. And so India is building on this architecture, which I don't know if you guys have spoken about, which is the consent-based architecture, where you can only get access to the records with consent. And India's consent-based architecture is also very granular in the sense that the patient will have control over who they're giving access records to, which records they're giving access to, how long, and even after they have given it, if they change their mind, they have the right to revoke it and remove access to your record. So that's the power of the system that's being put behind it. So one more level of detail for people who are tech-interested to understand how does this actually work. It's quite simple. So the National Health Authority, they are running this digital public, I should not use the word G there, digital public infrastructure for the country. And you'll see there are four boxes that you see there. The one on the top we have understood, that's basically the health account system, where people can have a health account. These two are registries. Have you guys covered, spoken about registries as a concept here? Yeah. So comes to the simple question, can anybody add information to my ABHA? Who can add information to my ABHA? Who do you think should be allowed to add information to my ABHA? So clearly, you want only trusted entities to add information to your ABHA. And how do you create trust? How do you know that this is really a hospital, this is really a doctor? So we do that through the idea of registries. So we have two registries. We have something called the health professional registry, which ultimately is going to cover. So again, it's got multiple purposes. So for example, India cannot answer the question today as to say, how many oncologists, surgical oncologists of a certain specialty are in a certain place, right? So it's able to answer such questions, but also to be able to build trust in terms of saying, hey, you are really the entity that you are and you can participate in this digital ecosystem. So how does it start? As a user, we are expecting you as a patient to be able to first get yourself an ABHA. How do you do that? You do that with any PHR app or you can get it when you go to a hospital. And what do we expect you to do with it? We expect you to share it with the healthcare provider when you visit the hospital. So you can share it by just doing the scan and share I spoke about in the previous slide or you show your card to the registration counter which scans the card is the two ways in which you can kind of do it. So the hospital basically gets now information about you and your health account and then in they, we expect the hospital to now use a software that's been modified to be what we call as ABDM compliant. And what that software does when it creates a new health information for you is that it links it with your ABHA. And the moment it links it with your ABHA, you will get a notification on your phone saying, hey, you have a new health record that's been added to your ABHA and you can actually view it on your phone. And not only can you view it later on if you go to another hospital or if they ask you, you can actually share it back again with your consent. And that's really the architecture that we're building and we're building this for national scale. This approach, the health information continues to stay here. And it's only passed between this entity and the user or the other facility. So we call it a federated architecture. The government itself and no health information goes through, even though we use the word, it's a health information exchange, but the actual exchange does not pass through it. Even if any information passes through, it's encrypted. So it's by design, it's privacy by design, because we do not want even the government who's operating these DPIs to be able to peek into or have access to any health information without the user's consent. And that's the crux of these ideas and this architecture. So how did we get here? For people who are interested in, you know, the journey to create digital public goods in your own country, it's usually a long process, at least for us in healthcare it was. We started by writing these ideas down that India should do digital public infrastructure in healthcare. We published that as a document. That happened in July 2018. The publication was done by India's planning department. And then they said, they put it out for public consultation, they got very, very positive feedback from the market. And so they said, oh, okay, this looks interesting. Let's create a committee to see how to make this real. So the second item that happened was this committee which said, what does it take to implement something like this? And one of the recommendations of the committee was that, hey, you need to give money and you need to have a department and a strong technology team if you want to do something like this. And that was identified to be the National Health Authority. So they gave the budget and the money in the middle of COVID, June 2020. They also asked us to launch it in three months, which we couldn't. But we did manage to get a pilot going by September 2020. And then we really managed to get to national rollout only by 2021. So but still a long journey. I should tell you that people like me have been working on this. I started 2016 on the first concepts, but it really took, you know, this is the first thing that we have to show is in 2018, really on terms of the screen. So and it's not done. You know, I think it'll take another decade because I'll show you the challenges in terms of actually bringing this to life because what we have brought to life is the digital public goods. The core challenge is actually getting ecosystem adoption in something like healthcare. Now it's very clear that if we have to make this happen, we have to get and I just maybe want to go back to, I'll use this slide and then come back. So how do we actually get the ecosystem to participate? What does it take? And in healthcare and every probably every digital public infrastructure or every sector is slightly different. But in this case, the challenge that we have is going back to our 200,000 health facilities, how do you convince all these guys to participate? So one of our biggest issues is that we do not have strong laws and regulators who oversee it. For example, with financial payments, you have the RBI, which acts as a very strong regulator. India is a federal structure. There's absolutely nobody. I, by the way, one of the things I do, apart from working as an advisor to the government is I run a chain of eye hospitals and I can tell you that there is absolutely no regulators who sit on our heads and can push us to do or push the healthcare industry to make changes. So that's one of the biggest problems that we have. But even before we can convince them, we need to prepare the second layer, the software layer. If you saw the previous diagram, you'll know that we need to convince these guys to basically integrate with all of these digital public goods. So they need, if they have a version 1 of their software or version X of their software, we need an X plus 1 that is integrated and compliant. And so that's our first big challenge. That is, we need to get all of these guys on board and then we need to convince them to go back, roll it out in those facilities as part of the overall thing. So, so far, out of the 2000 vendors, we have convinced about 100 to complete this process. And how do we do it? We actually have something that we have created called the ABDM sandbox. So the ABDM sandbox is like an environment where we host the digital public infrastructure and we invite parties to come in, integrate with the API. So we put all the documentation out, we put the specification out, we have a team to hand hold and explain. We run webinars to all of these development companies saying, what does it mean to make your software ABDM compliant? And how do you actually integrate, test, comply, make sure that the security is solid. And after the end of the process, we give them a key, actually a production key, which gives them access to the production digital public infrastructure. So you cannot get access to the production digital public infrastructure unless you can clear the sandbox process. And that's really the thing the National Health Authority runs. And that's the primary way with which it's trying to create this engagement with the ecosystem. Of course, just by putting up a sandbox, you don't get it. So we are also doing two other things. I don't have slides for it. I do have slides in the other deck. But one, we are giving away money now. So we basically said, hey, rupees 20 for every health record you link with an ABA account for the next X period of time. And we are giving that money to the healthcare provider and we are giving money to the software provider. So it's 20 rupees to the healthcare provider, 5 rupees to the software vendor who helped him do it, so that we can actually kick start and get the ecosystem doing. The second thing is, we are trying to make sure that this aligns. So India just passed its data privacy laws. And one of the things that the data privacy laws is, of course, health data gets covered under the data privacy laws. One of the things that is required is that all healthcare folks have to comply. And what we are trying to do is we are making sure that if you deploy an ABDM compliant software in your hospital or clinic, that's going to make it the easiest way for you to comply with the data protection bill. So those are the kind of incentives that we are driving to do for adoption. We have also broken up the ecosystem into three groups. We are talking separately to people who build software for the government, people who build software for the large corporate hospitals, and also people who build software for small clinics and labs, because we found that there are three different kind of class of groups that you need to engage with in terms of how we are doing this. So that's our journey in healthcare so far. And those are some results. So on a population of about what we are now 1.4 billion, we have managed to kind of crore is about that's about 10. I don't expect you to get on to Airtel to take my call or receive my call or call me. All the population scale infrastructure systems like internet, telecom, which has billions and billions of people, are fundamentally interoperable. Pramod mentioned telecom has a protocol called GSM which is the language for two telecom systems, machines to understand voice, exchange voice or data. Internet has HTTP protocol which makes anybody who can make a browser, anybody can put up a website, they talk to each other. Those two applications can talk to each other. Telephones has a protocol called as SMTP which makes any two email servers talk to each other. There is no one person in the center trying to control this coordination between a sender and receiver. Well, that is not the case with WhatsApp because you got to be on WhatsApp for a person to talk to. If the other person is not on WhatsApp, you cannot have this conversation. Now which is where we said is it possible, very simple question we have been asking, if money with UPI can go from any app to any app, I can be on phone pay but I can make a money transfer to Paytm, if UPI was a protocol, why is not commerce like UPI, like email, like telecom? Why just commerce? Why cannot education, online education be like emails or telecom? Why cannot energy, discovering and EV charging be like that? Now what does it do? It fundamentally, it fundamentally opens up the possibility of, the point that Pramod mentioned right, you do not need one Uber or one Airtel or one Amazon to solve the problem at scale. If you create a DPI infrastructure, in this case an open network like the internet, what are you doing? You are not solving it by itself, you are distributing that ability to solve. So what is happening in a network like ONDC or in a network like Namayatri or in this example of an EV charging network is that you are breaking the problem, splitting the needs of the problem into multiple sets, so you have one platform that has the consumer, it does not have to worry about where the provider is, there are whole set of other platforms like a Namayatri taxi app or an EV charging company or a retail store on an online app like Shopify, each solving for their ends of the users and then they coming and discovering each other without having to be on each other's platform registered. Being on WhatsApp, I am able to discover 80 different charging companies, charging points in my neighborhood without having to be registered on their platforms. In Bangalore, we have about 200,000 drivers, 200,000, 2 lakh drivers who have chosen to not use Uber but get on to Namayatri in this decentralized peer-to-peer. So when you unbundle and put consumer on a different platform and a provider on a different platform and you make them talk, you get what a gentleman just said, a decentralized peer-to-peer network. So if e-commerce is not, think of commerce not as a platform, think of EV charging not as a platform, think of teleconsultation not as a platform but start thinking of it as open networks which is what internet is, only difference is internet solve for exchange of information which is a content web, what Beckin is trying to do is to try to create the transaction web for any economic transaction. Now probably this is my last two minutes and then come here, I will take questions and then I will hand it over to Kamya. What happens in a typical economic transaction? What happens in a ride booking? What happens in an online purchase or in an EV discoverability? There are four steps that fundamentally happen in any economic transaction life cycle. One is you are searching for something, say I want to find an EV charging, I want to find a taxi driver, I want to find a doctor or I want to find a teacher in my neighborhood, I want to find an advice for my crop which is what you just heard from JB, that's the discovery phase and then you find something, one is good, can I find the EV charger, can I find the driver, can I place an order without having to be on that person's platform, can I book order that resource, can I block the EV charger, can I buy that advice, can I get the taxi driver, can I book that ride, that's the order part. The third part is, is the order getting fulfilled, is the service actually getting delivered, whether it's a service or a goods, in ONDC you are delivering even grocery, the goods getting delivered, that's the fulfillment part. The fourth part in this sort of a DNA strand is the post fulfillment, not always the service is fulfilled effectively as per expectations, you may want to do cancellation, you may want to do return or refund or even raise a grievance or even rate the service or the service provider there are these four blocks. Now surprisingly and I want you guys to ask this question, think of any sector where there is an economic transaction between any consumer and provider, I give you an example of EV, think of mobility or any sector in your imagination, even manufacturing, you know just humor me on this, you will always see that DOFP, discovery order fulfillment, post fulfillment will be the essential sort of the DNA strand of that transaction. Beckin has generalized that just like how UPI was a one and a half page, we couldn't make it one and a half, we made it two and a half with DOFP, so it's a two and a half page specification, a set of vocabulary, it's not a platform, it's just a bunch of vocabulary that machines understand to execute a discovery order fulfillment, post fulfillment for any economic resource, yeah. What you didn't see in that video in the interest of time, maybe we can share it later, is that it doesn't stop at EV discovery, that EV charging company itself, where does it buy energy from today? The energy is trying to buy from the grid, now with this network in India, nowhere else in the world this is happening, in India we have a way to discover energy from an idle battery, which is solar powered, which means green energy, imagine a school bus lying, electric school bus lying idle, right, it just probably runs four hours a day and it's lying idle, but it's a fully charged battery, how do I monetize an idle battery? Is energy a resource, an idle battery an economic resource, can I put a DOFP on that? So EV charging company is able to put a DOFP on an idle battery and buy green energy at a lesser price, because that is a price for an idle battery, right, it would have otherwise not have been monetized. So in India we have a possibility because of interoperability and open network that you will be able to buy energy from an EV charging station which is green at a price lesser than the grid energy. I don't have to explain if this is the possibility, why will market not adopt it, right, because there is a natural incentive for private innovators to jump on to this infrastructure like how many companies like Amazon jumped on to a network like internet to reimagine how we buy things. Open network is creating that possibility for players to now start imagining should I play the double sided game of bringing the consumer and provider on to my platform or rather expand the pie as Pramod said by playing one side of the game but playing it very well and offer things that we can't even imagine today. There is nothing in the design of internet that would have told you that there will be a charge GPT after 50 years. That is the power of generalization that can unleash a whole lot of innovation. So I can't even sit and imagine what an open network can unlock tomorrow. One of the, I will probably start with this whimsical example and imagination that I often share, you are in Kormangala, okay, this is like the worst traffic hell out there in the world in terms of getting anywhere out. Uber doesn't know that somebody is getting served coffee inside the car, Starbucks doesn't know that the guy is actually in the car who is getting the coffee, but somebody else like a Google map or a Paytm using backend protocol on ONDC can make this happen.
